Preface
This master thesis project was prepared in fulﬁllment of the requirements for a degree in Master
of Science at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The Project has been supervised by senior researcher Igor Kozin (DTU Management Engi-
neering), risk analyst J. Robert Taylor (DTU Management Engineering), associate Professor
Poul Andersen (Department of Mechanical Engineering) and risk analyst Rikke Aarøe Carlsen
(Ready?). We would like to show our utmost appreciation for their help and supervision through
the endeavor of this thesis and extend a special thanks to Igor Kozin and J. Robert Taylor for
their cooperation during this process, it has been highly appreciated.
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The objectives of this thesis were to describe and understand the causes and development of ﬁres
on container ships from a safety engineering point of view and to provide a basis for evaluating
solutions to the problem of detecting ﬁres.
In light of the objectives the thesis was initialized by putting a perspective on container ship ﬁres
by studying: the evolution of container ships, containerized transport of dangerous goods, ﬁre
detection arrangements, intermodal shipping container ﬁre safety and the ﬁre ﬁghting arrange-
ments aboard container ships. This section served as the basis for understanding the extensive
increase in container ship sizes and the associated ﬁre related risk.
To describe and understand the causes and development of ﬁres, a ﬁre scenario and hazard
identiﬁcation study was conducted. Through the ﬁre scenario study the authors were able to
identify 36 container ship ﬁres between 1996 - 2017. The ﬁndings from the hazard identiﬁcation
study led the authors to conclude, calcium hypochlorite, compressed charcoal briquette products,
rechargeable batteries and divinylbenzene, to constitute the main hazards to the cargo related ﬁre
safety aboard container ships.
To explore the cause consequence relations associated with the containerized shipment and
storage of the identiﬁed hazards a cause consequence study was conducted. One parallel could
be drawn between the potential causes for the ignition of the cargo ﬁres, namely the cargoes
sensitivity to heat exposure during containerized storage. From the consequence part of the study
it was possible to discover an event line from the initiation process of a critical ﬁre event to
the ﬁnal event, for all of the identiﬁed hazards and the development process of the ﬁres during
these chains of events. In all the investigated real-life ﬁre scenarios, premature termination of
the voyage was unavoidable and external ﬁreﬁghting assistance was necessary to successfully
terminate the ﬁres. Extensive damage was in all ﬁre scenarios inﬂicted to the containerized cargo
and/or ship. Crew members lost their lives in two of the explored ﬁre incidents. In all of the ﬁre
incidents a direct parallel could be drawn between the inadequacy in the response time of the ﬁre
detection and/or reliability and effectiveness of the ﬁreﬁghting arrangements and the severity of
the damage inﬂicted.
==PAGE:5==
Through a frequency study it was determined that the frequency at which high consequence
cargo ﬁres occur aboard container ships, was 6.86·10−4 per ship year, with a 90% chance that
the actual frequency was within the interval 4.56·10−4 and 9.94·10−4, in the period 2011-2016.
Based on these ﬁndings a solution study was conducted to explore a solution space of ideas
which could potentiate the ﬁrst safety barrier in the protection against ﬁres, namely ﬁre detection.
Through this study two preliminary concepts, were considered worth further exploring.
[1]The ﬁrst idea was to place temperature sensors in or on all shipping containers
[2] The second idea was to install an optical smoke detector i all containers.
In the attempt of providing a basis for evaluating the solutions’ actual worth to the problem
of detecting ﬁres, a ﬁre simulation study was conducted by applying the computational ﬂuid
dynamic software Fire Dynamic Simulator. The aim of the study was to simulate a ﬁre igniting
within a sealed container, during storage in the bottom of a cargo hold ﬁlled with containers. The
study showed that the temperature sensor positioned within the container was able to detect a
temperature increase, approximately 27 seconds after the ignition of the ﬁre. This constitutes an
improvement in detection response time from the simulated Sample Extraction Smoke Detection
system (a method applied on container ships for smoke detection) of minimum 100% (Detection
interval from SESD detection points: 60 seconds - 74 seconds). It was further discovered that by
placing the optical smoke detector within the container, smoke was detected within 30 seconds.

==PAGE:9==
1. Introduction
Liner shipping is a global trading industry, which in 2015 was estimated to be responsible for
transporting approximately 8.428 billion ton-miles of the world’s seaborne trade via containers.
The industry’s vast size, great market share and high importance for international trade, has
resulted in an industry competing on capacity, economics and efﬁciency. As a consequence of
these competitive factors, an exponential growth in container ship sizes and their cargo carrying
capacities has been witnessed throughout the last ﬁve decades. The main driver for this evolution
has been to achieve economies of scale.
However, the evolution within ﬁreﬁghting capabilities of container ships has not followed
the same tendencies through the last decades. This has left the crew onboard container ships
with limited possibilities of detecting and ﬁghting ﬁres. As the throughput of containers in port
terminals has increased as well, the feasibility of physically investigating the content of each
container has been decreased signiﬁcantly. The probability of unknowingly transporting and
handling undeclared dangerous goods has therefore increased.
Considering the above issues, the objective of this thesis is:
• The 1st objective: To describe and understand the causes and development of ﬁres on
container ships from a safety engineering point of view
• The 2nd objective: To provide a basis for evaluating solutions to the problem of detecting
ﬁres.
The thesis will be initialized by introducing the reader to the issues surrounding the ship-
ment of intermodal shipping containers by container ships, seen from ﬁre safety point of view.
This current section will focus on: the evolution of container ships, containerized transport of
dangerous goods, ﬁre detection arrangements, intermodal shipping container ﬁre safety and the
ﬁre ﬁghting arrangements aboard the container ships.
As the shipment of containerized cargo by sea imposes a series of hazards to container ships, its
crew and the environment, a ﬁre scenario and hazard identiﬁcation study will be conducted. The
main objective of this section is to investigate and classify the substances and/or articles which
imposes the main hazards to the ﬁre safety aboard container ships. Here extensive in-depth
analysis of documented past container ship ﬁre cases and their apparent causes will be presented.
==PAGE:10==
10
Chapter 1. Introduction
To explore the cause consequence relations associated with the containerized shipment of the
identiﬁed hazards and the development paths of these ﬁres, a cause consequence study will be
performed.
To determine the actual frequency at which cargo ﬁres occur aboard container ships and potential
solutions to the problem of detecting ﬁres, a frequency study and solution study will be con-
ducted and presented. In order to provide a basis for evaluating the attained solution space, a
ﬁre development study will be conducted which will serve as the last section of this thesis. The
ﬁre simulation study will be conducted through the computational ﬂuid dynamic software ﬁre
dynamic simulator.
Growth of container ships cargo carrying capacity
The main reason behind the growth in container ships cargo-carrying capacities, has been
to achieve economies of scale. The principle "economies of scale" is the attempt to reduce
operational costs (cost per transported Twenty-Foot Equivalent unit (TEU) ) by increasing the
capacity of the ship. By increasing the cargo-carrying capabilities of the ship, while reducing the
fuel consumption, manpower requirement and other operational expenditures, the total-cost of
ownership can be reduced over the ships operational life-time. An overview of the growth rate in
the container ships cargo-carrying capabilities, throughout the last ﬁve decades, is given in ﬁgure
1.1, [Allianz, 2015].
Figure 1.1: Container ship capacity growth from from 1968 - 2018
Major cargo container related ﬁres aboard container ships
In parallel to the growth of container ships cargo carrying capacity, a number of cargo ﬁre related
incidents have been seen. During the period of 1996 to 2017, 36 major cargo related ﬁres were
found to have happened. These have been listed in table 1.1 and will create the basis for the
studies conducted throughout this thesis. The applied method for attaining the data presented in
the table, will be elaborated throughout the section of ﬁre scenarios and hazard identiﬁcation.

Scope of the study
The far larger problems of preventing cargo container ﬁres involves complicated organizational
studies and measures, which are beyond the scope of this thesis and in any case needs to be based
on a thorough understanding of ﬁre development. This thesis will focus on the basic concepts of
ﬁres and the development process of a cargo ﬁre igniting within a sealed shipping container.
==PAGE:12==
2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
The following sections will be dealing with the preliminary investigations on cargo container ﬁres
aboard container ships. These will serve as the basis for understanding the issues surrounding
the extensive increase in the size of container ships and the ﬁre related risk. Initially the numbers
describing the increase in size are presented. Hereafter recent research on the economical conse-
quences of a total loss of an ultra large container vessel are described, which leads to a general
introduction and discussion of the actual difﬁculties facing the crews and shipowners when
transporting cargo containers. This includes the whole issue of being subjected to undeclared
dangerous good, the limitations of ﬁre/smoke detection in cargo holds and what the crew is
subjected to when performing ﬁre ﬁghting. As will be seen, ﬁres are a signiﬁcant problem for
container ships.
2.1
Container ships
The idea of transporting containerized cargo by sea, was initially introduced in the early 1950s
by the American transport entrepreneur Malcom Purcell McLean. Mr. Mclean pioneered the
container shipping industry in 1956, with his ship the Ideal X, transporting 58 metal container
boxes and 15,000 tons of bulk petroleum, from Port Newark to Houston in the USA. The
Ideal X is considered to be the worlds ﬁrst container ship and the starting point of today’s
modern containerized trade. The introduction of the container ship revolutionized international
trade, which as a consequence resulted in a rapid structural evolution in the container ship
building industry in order to support the exponentially growing containerization and industrial
globalization.1 Since its origin, the container ship has gone through 8 major structural eras,
summarized in ﬁgure 2.1.
1http://www.worldshipping.org/about-the-industry/history-of-containerization/the-birth-of-intermodalism
==PAGE:13==
2.1 Container ships
13
Figure 2.1: The evolution of the container ships
Source: Food + City
The early container ships, were constructed from converted cargo ships and tankers, struc-
turally reinforced to withstand the load of up to 800 TEU of containerized cargo. The ﬁrst fully
cellular container ships were introduced in the early 1970s, specially designed for the purpose of
efﬁcient transport and storage of stacked containers alone. These ships were designed to hold
a capacity of 1.000-2.500 TEU and were the ﬁrst ships ever to apply the whole ship for the
storage of stacked containers. Since the 1970s, economies of scale has escalated the increase
in container ship sizes, which through 7 generations of container ships and only 50 years of
building container ships, has created the design basis for what is today known as the ultra large
container ship (ULCS) [Rodrigue, 2012]. The deﬁnition ULCS, describes a design generation of
container ships with capacities of 10.000 or more TEU. The construction of such ships, comes
with increased challenges in terms of their designs where resistance and propulsion, structural
assessments, vibrations, hydrodynamics and powering are some of the main challenges that has
be contended with.
Besides above challenges, the increase in container ship sizes have been accompanied by
as rising concern regarding increased risk, safety concerns and the potential for higher monetary
expenditures in the event of experiencing an accident which could lead to a total loss of a ULCS.
Allianz Global Corporate & Specialty issued a report in 2016 named "Safety and Shipping
Review 2016" [Allianz, 2016] which provides an annual review of trends and developments in
shipping losses and safety. The report contains a case study, estimating the monetary losses in
the event of a new 19.000 TEU container ship (80% laden) capsizing, leading to a total loss of
the ship, cargo and consequently a salvage operation to remove the wreck. In order to put the
capacity of the ship into perspective, this would equal the loss of one of the worlds currently
largest ULCS, MSC Oscar, which is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.2.
==PAGE:14==
14
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
Figure 2.2: The ultra large container ship MSC Oscar, carriage capability: 19.224 TEU
Source: wikipedia
A categorized monetary loss overview is given in table 2.1. The ﬁgures are based on the
study conducted by [Allianz, 2016].
Table 2.1: Monetary expenditures in case of a total loss of a 19.000 TEU container ship (80%
laden)
Description
Expenditure
Hull loss
(Insured value) $200 millions
Cargo loss
Approx. $532 millions
Removal of wreck and liabilities
Approx. $300 millions
Total loss
Approx. $1 billion
The study shows that a total loss of one of the largest ULCS in the world, could result in a
total monetary loss of $1 billion.
The MSC Oscar, was built and delivered by Daewoo Shipbuilding & Marine Engineering
(DSME) from South Korea in January 2015 and was acquired by MSC Mediterranean Shipping
Company at a cost of $140 million.2 The threat of loosing a ULCS like MSC Oscar, therefore
imposes a monetary risk, approximately seven times greater than the initial capital expenditures
spent on acquiring it.
But what could be the driver for such an event to occur?
One of the main concerns, seen from a safety engineering point of view, is that today’s ULCS are
build with the same basic design principles, as the older smaller container ships, without concern
of the possible consequences this increased size could inﬂict on the industry. It is generally
2https://www.msc.com/sur/press/press-releases/2015-march/msc-oscar-world-s-largest-container-ship1-makes-
ma
==PAGE:15==
2.2 Dangerous goods
15
assumed that safety principles invented and applied decades ago for ships of much smaller
capacity and sizes, will work with the same efﬁciency and effectiveness on today’s signiﬁcantly
larger ships. One of these principles is the ﬁre safety and ﬁre safety arrangements, which will be
the main focus of this thesis.
In order to put a perspective on the ﬁre safety and ﬁre safety arrangements currently applied
on ULCS’s, one initially needs to explore the regulatory framework for container sea transport.
Therefore each of the following sections in this chapter will be initialized with an introduction to
the rules and regulations which forms the framework for the general ﬁre safety at sea. Each of
these sections will be followed by an elaboration on the ﬁre related safety implications of these
principles, seen from a safety engineering perspective.
2.2
Dangerous goods
The term "dangerous goods" is a classiﬁcation of substances or articles which have the potential
to become a hazard to the safety of human life, ship and/or the environment. These types of
goods can exist in three states of matter; liquid, solid and gas. Each can present a varying
range of hazards when being subjected to containerized transport e.g. ﬂammable and/or toxic
(poisonous). The transport of cargo designated as dangerous goods is an essential part of
international trade and world progress. The reason being that many of these types of cargo are
necessary when manufacturing other products such as pharmaceuticals, computers, cleaning
products, etc. Depending on the state of matter and properties of these cargoes, different rules
and regulations are prescribed in regards to packing, handling and transport. These rules and
regulations are formally set and presented in the International Maritime Dangerous Goods
(IMDG) Code [Technologies, 2014].
2.2.1
Regulatory framework - IMDG Code
The IMDG code is the legal framework for marine transport of dangerous goods and marine
pollutants, applicable for all cargo-carrying ships. The IMDG code was initially published in
1965 by the International Maritime Organization (IMO) . IMO, established in 1959, is the main
organization, designated by the United Nations (UN) to regulate shipping. The code consists of
7 parts, which are presented in two volumes, listed in table 2.2 and 2.3.
Volume 1[Technologies, 2014]
Table 2.2: Overview and introduction to volume 1 of the IMDG code
Part
Description
1
General provisions, deﬁnitions and training
2
Classiﬁcation
3
Packing and Tank Provisions
5
Consignment Procedures
6
Construction and Testing of Packagings, Intermediate Bulk Containers (IBCs), Large Packagings,
Portable Tanks, Multiple Element Gas Containers (MEGCs) and Road Tank Vehicles
7
Requirements Concerning Transport Operations
==PAGE:16==

Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires

The IMDG code was made a mandatory requirement January 1st 2004, for all signatory
nations of the International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea(SOLAS) . For the purpose
of simpliﬁcation, only the most relevant of the rules and regulations for shipping containerized
dangerous goods are presented.
The IMDG code contains nine categories in which the dangerous goods are classed, based
on the hazard they present. For each category, a unique label is assigned for the purpose of
visual identiﬁcation of the dangers the respective transported cargo induce. Table 2.4 presents an
overview of all classes and their respective hazards.
Table 2.4: IMDG classiﬁcations of dangerous goods and the respective dangers they impose
Class
Hazard
1
Explosive
2
Gases
3
Flammable liquids
4
Flammable solids and other ﬂammable substances
5
Oxidizing substances and organic peroxides
6
Toxic and infectious substances
7
Radioactive material
8
Corrosive substances
9
Miscellaneous dangerous substances and articles
Some of the classiﬁcations presented in table 2.4, contain sub-divisions speciﬁc for high risk-
and low risk categorization. For each of the classiﬁcations, certain requirements are to be met in
order to insure safe transport by sea.
The IMDG code requires the dangerous goods be correctly and safely;
• Classiﬁed and identiﬁed
• Packed
• Marked, labelled and placarded
• Documented
• Stowed on board the ship
• Segregated from other goods which they may react hazardously with
Proper training is required for all crews involved in the shipment of dangerous goods by sea
and further information about the hazardous cargo must be made available for the purpose of
appropriate emergency response.
==PAGE:17==
2.2 Dangerous goods
17
2.2.2
Safety implications of dangerous goods
The risk of ﬁre is not a new threat, but one which has increased in parallel with the progression of
the increased container ship sizes. The worlds largest container ship currently measure a capacity
of approximately 21.000 TEU. The rapid growth in the ships container carrying capacity, has
increased the demand for containers being transshipped during port-visits. In order to keep up
schedule integrity and improving ship asset utilization the ship-owners continuously try to reduce
the time spent in port by the ships. As a consequence, the port terminals struggle to cope with
the growing demand and the large numbers of containers being on- and off-loaded within the
restricted time frame.
The limited transit time per loaded container, makes it difﬁcult to check the content of each
container stowed on board the ship. This increases the likelihood of transporting mis-declared
dangerous goods, as the ship’s crew has to rely on the manifest provided by the shipper of
the cargo. As elaborated in section 2.2.1, the IMDG code contains strict provisions for the
safe carriage of dangerous goods by sea. If these requirements are not properly met, the prob-
ability of these hazards evolving into undesirable events e.g. ﬁres and/or explosions are increased.
An example could be a container carrying mis-declared calcium hypochlorite. Calcium hypochlo-
rite is a chemical commonly applied for disinfection of drinking water, swimming pool water
or applied as an ingredient in some household cleaning products. Calcium hypochlorite has a
history of causing very serious on-board ﬁres and explosions, due to its unstable and self-reactive
chemical nature [Surveys, 2016].
This observation is backed by investigations conducted by, the International Group of Pro-
tection and Indemnity Clubs (IGP & I) :
The IG Clubs have, in recent years, observed an apparent upsurge in container ﬁres involving
Calcium Hypochlorite, leading to some lines imposing very strict carriage precautions or even
outright carriage bans. Fire incident investigations also suggest that, in the vast majority of
cases, the Calcium Hypochlorite has been either mis-declared by shippers (and therefore wrongly
described as a product that would ordinarily be considered harmless); or having less strict
carriage requirements under the IMDG Code.[IGP&I, 2016]
Calcium Hypochlorite requires careful handling and proper storage (preferably storage in a
reefer container), whereas improper handling, storage and/or segregation from combustible
solids, liquids or other types of goods could lead to a serious ﬁres or in worst case an explosion
on the ship.
Above empathizes the importance of the rules and regulations set by the IMDG code and
how the shipment of mis-declared dangerous goods can increase the risk of ﬁre.
The storage of mis-declared dangerous goods does not only increase the threat of experiencing a
ﬁre during voyage, but further decreases the efﬁciency and likelihood of extinguishing such ﬁres.
The IMDG code requires that appropriate emergency response information is made available,
for the transported dangerous goods. If this information is not available and the crew does not
know what type of cargo caused the ﬁre, they have limited chance of applying appropriate and
effective extinguishing methods. This could results in a high risk ﬁreﬁghting response, where the
application of inadequate or wrongful suppression methods could increase the overall harmful
consequences to the crew and ship.
==PAGE:18==
18
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
2.3
Fire Detection Arrangements
Fire detection is an essential part of the ﬁre safety and ﬁre safety arrangements on board ULCSs
as it presents the ﬁrst safety barrier in the protection against ﬁres, with the purpose of reducing
the consequences of them. Detection time is deﬁned as the time it takes from the point of ignition,
until the ﬁrst crew member gets notice of the ﬁre related incident. The efﬁciency of the methods
of detecting ﬁres on ULCSs, can make the difference between being able to extinguish a ﬁre or
not. The rules and regulations which deﬁnes the ﬁre detection requirements on board ULCSs are
deﬁned and formulated by the International Convention for the Safety of Life at Sea (SOLAS).
2.3.1
Regulatory framework - SOLAS
The ﬁrst convention on required safety equipment and construction was SOLAS 1914, set in place
after Titanic sank. In the regulations several initiatives were adopted concerning watertight and
ﬁre-resistant bulkheads, lifesaving appliances, ﬁre prevention and ﬁre ﬁghting. The regulations
were only adopted/signed by 5 states, but acted as a cornerstone for future regulations. Following
the ﬁrst edition of SOLAS came SOLAS 1929 and 1948. SOLAS 1929 implemented new rules
on navigation, lifesaving equipment, ﬁre prevention and ﬁre ﬁghting, while SOLAS 1948 was
simply a revisions of SOLAS 1929 to accommodate the technical development entailed by the
Second World War. It also covered a wider range of ships and described implementation of
safety in greater detail.
In 1959 SOLAS was updated yet again, this resulted in SOLAS 1960. Steps were taken
to improve the ability to periodically update the convention. However, this did not work properly
in practice and therefore an entirely new convention was implemented via SOLAS 1974. SOLAS
1974 contained an article enabling amendments to be applied within an acceptable time-frame.
With this article in place SOLAS 1974 has not been change since, but regulations have been
updated and expanded regularly to keep up with technical advances and to implement lesson
learned from accidents.3
SOLAS chapter II-2- Fire protection, ﬁre detection and ﬁre extinction, describes the current inter-
national regulations on ﬁre safety on ships. Below is a presentation of "Regulation 7 Detection
and alarm", speciﬁc for cargo ships
2 General requirements:
2.1 A ﬁxed ﬁre detection and ﬁre alarm system shall be provided in accordance with the provi-
sions of this regulation.
2.2 A ﬁxed ﬁre detection and ﬁre alarm system and a sample extraction smoke detection system
required in this regulation and other regulations in this part shall be of an approved type and
comply with the Fire Safety Systems Code.
2.3 Where a ﬁxed ﬁre detection and ﬁre alarm system is required for the protection of spaces
other than those speciﬁed in paragraph 5.1, at least one detector complying with the Fire Safety
Systems Code shall be installed in each such space. 4

life-at-sea-(solas),-1974.aspx

==PAGE:19==
2.3 Fire Detection Arrangements
19
2.3.2
Safety implications of current ﬁre detection methods
Currently there are no requirements for the installation ﬁxed ﬁre detection systems, above the
weather deck of a container ship. Therefore the ships ﬁre safety relies solely on visual detection
from the crew. The reliability of this method is depended on the overview/visibility of the
containerized cargo from the bridge, weather conditions, location of the container in question
(i.e. line of sight to the affected container), general crew rounds, container inspections during
voyage etc.. Due to the increased capacity of the ULCSs and the tight stowage of containers, the
overview of the containerized cargo is restricted, which may impair the efﬁciency of this method
for detecting ﬁres.
Figure 2.3 and 2.4, display a longitudinal and transverse proﬁle of a 13.100 TEU container ship
and gives a perspective on the amount of containers that the a typical crew of approximately
12-20 people is required to observe during voyage. It needs to be considered that the worlds
largest container ships are able to carry 6.000-8000 TEU more than the displayed container ship.
Figure 2.3: General arrangement of a 13.100 TEU Container carrier - longitudinal proﬁle
Figure 2.4: General arrangement of a 13.100 TEU Container carrier - transverse proﬁle
This method of detection therefore puts a high responsibility on the crew and simultaneously
increases the workload of the individual crew members. A concern is that the increased size of
the container ships has not led to an increase in crew sizes, on the contrary it is the opposite.
The new ULCS operate with new and highly automated systems, which have decreased the need
for manual labor. The worlds second largest container ship, MSC Oscar, can accommodate
a maximum of 35 crew members, but often operates with much smaller crews. From a ﬁre
protection point of view, the increased and tight on-deck storage of containerized cargo combined
==PAGE:20==
20
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
with the decrease in manning, may prolong the detection time.
The increased quantity of containers stowed on the deck of the container ships also means
an increased amount of containers carrying dangerous goods that the crew are required to deal
with. The cargo diversity which falls within this categorization and the individual ﬁre hazards
these types of cargo induce, makes them an signiﬁcant risk to the ﬁre safety and is often not
only the originating factor, but also the reason a ﬁre is able evolve from a sealed container. The
continuous need for observation makes it difﬁcult for the crew to keep track of such a high
quantity of containers. This poses a considerable threat to the safety of the ship and crew, as the
reaction time is a crucial parameter for the likelihood of extinguishing a ﬁre. The longer time it
takes to detect an emerging ﬁre, the bigger the chance is that the crew is not able to extinguish or
even contain it.
Another issue with relying on visual detection as the only method of detecting ﬁres above
the weather deck, is the visual barrier created by the container itself. As a consequence of the
way a container hermetically seals the cargo, visual detection of an emerging interior ﬁre is
almost impossible. As a results, most on-board ﬁres emerging in containers above the weather
deck, are not detected before a container starts to emit smoke or structural fatigue begins to show
on the container casing.
The emission of smoke is likewise the main component for detecting ﬁres in the cargo holds
below the weather deck. Regulations requires a minimum of one smoke detector to be placed
in each cargo hold or a sample extraction smoke detection (SESD) system , which sequentially
measures the amount of smoke in the cargo space through air samples. These systems are
designed to sound an alarm immediately after the detection of smoke in order to alert to the
presence of ﬁre, thereby providing the opportunity for an early suppression response. Smoke
detectors and SESD systems are commonly applied in many industries and have proven to be
very effective as a method of detecting ﬁres. The issue with applying smoke detectors or SESD
system as the main source for detecting ﬁres in cargo holds, does not lie within the effectiveness
of the systems themselves. Rather, this method of detection and number of required detectors,
might no longer be sufﬁcient as a consequence of the increased size of container ships and
thereby the volumetric increase in the size of the individual cargo holds.
The 13.100 TEU container ship displayed in ﬁgure 2.3, contains a total 10 cargo holds within the
hull of the ship. The storage capabilities of the individual cargo holds vary depending on their
transverse placement in the hull. A cargo hold contains two container bays, that are separated by
an open transverse frame construction. Each of the cargo holds are separated by a transverse
watertight bulkhead as illustrated in ﬁgure 2.5.
==PAGE:21==
2.3 Fire Detection Arrangements
21
Figure 2.5: View into the cargo holds of a container ship. Source: wikipedia
The largest cargo holds are placed near the midship section of the container ship see ﬁgure
2.6 and 2.7 and measures, by rough calculations, 25,000 m3.
Figure 2.6: Transverse interior view to one
of the largest cargo holds on a 13.100 TEU
container ship
Figure 2.7: Longitudinal interior view to
one of the largest cargo holds on a 13.100
TEU container ship
A cargo hold of this size has an approximate capacity of 740 TEU, of close stored containers.
In case of experiencing an emerging interior ﬁre in one of these containers, the emission of
smoke is initially limited to the amount of air allowed through the small atmospheric vents
positioned along the container walls. This restricts the dispersion of smoke and thereby the
==PAGE:22==
22
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
emitted concentration of smoke per m3 of cargo space, in the initial phases of the ﬁre.
The average duration between, the smoke exiting from the container reaches a detectable level
and the ﬁre reaches its peak heat release rate (HRR) may vary depending on a number of factors;
The placement of the container in question, (i.e how far the container is stored from the actual
placement of the smoke detector, or the air sampling extraction points of the SESD system), the
size of the cargo hold, the cargo which initially caused the ﬁre.
In a cargo space with the size of 25,000 m3, the probability of a ﬁre not being detected be-
fore structural fatigue in the container casing allows a more heavy dispersion of smoke to be
emitted, is quite high. At the point the ﬂames pierce through the container shell, the available
oxygen within the cargo hold would cause the HRR to exponentially increase, reaching its peak
within minutes. The heat transfer from such a container ﬁre would likely cause the ﬁre to spread
to adjacent containers, at which point it might be too late for the crew to combat the ﬁre manually.
Another concern is that the smoke detection systems do not elaborate on the originating point
of the ﬁre or the magnitude of the detected ﬁre. This presents a considerable threat to the crew
and element of surprise, as they need to combat a ﬁre without knowing the origination point of
the ﬁre, what cargo caused the ﬁre to break out and further the extent of the ﬁre in a cargo hold
presumably full with hot toxic smoke.
Therefore the combination between the increased size in the cargo spaces and the current
requirements for the application of smoke detecting systems, might not be sufﬁcient due to the
increased size of the ships.
As initially explained, ﬁre detection forms the ﬁrst safety barrier in the protection against
ﬁre incidents and thereby constitutes an essential element in reducing the consequences of such
events. For this reason the authors of this thesis have taken a great interest in this matter. In order
to examine the current detection arrangements one initially needs to explore how a ﬁre develops
within a sealed container. To do so the authors will undertake studies applying computational
ﬂuid dynamics (CFD) to conduct such ﬁre simulations. The main objective of this study will
therefore be to investigate the escalation of a ﬁre within a container and investigate ﬁre detection
solutions that could increase the general ﬁre safety on board the ULCSs.
==PAGE:23==
2.4 Intermodal Shipping Container Fire Safety
23
2.4
Intermodal Shipping Container Fire Safety
Intermodal shipping containers can be segregated into 5 categories, which are general cargo,
thermal, tank, dry bulk and platform containers. The general cargo and thermal being the most
relevant to this thesis, as they are the most common and widely used types of containers utilised
throughout the industry.
The thermal container category includes all containers that have insulating material cover-
ing the walls, doors, ﬂoor and roof. A thermal container does not necessarily have a device
installed for cooling or heating. Some containers may for instance simply be ﬁlled with ice,
dry ice or liqueﬁed inert gas to keep the internal temperature sufﬁciently cooled for the entire
journey. However, the most commonly know thermal containers are reefer containers, which are
mechanically refrigerated via an integral refrigeration unit. These are used for several purposes
where the cargo requires a temperature controlled environment. This may be frozen agricultural
products, but in many cases it might as well be dangerous goods like self-reactive substances or
organic peroxides that are required to be transported under temperature control. An example of a
reefer container can be seen in ﬁgure 2.8.
Figure 2.8: A typical refrigerated container, also know as a reefer container. Source: The
Refrigerated Container Group
The general cargo container can usually be described as a steel box. These "steel boxes" are
built for speciﬁc purposes or general purposes. A speciﬁc purpose might be transportation of
green coffee beans, from Southern and Central America to the rest of the world. As the beans
require greater amounts of ventilation during transit than other cargo these containers have
ventilations channels along the whole length of the container’s top and bottom. For transportation
of more typical commodities like consumer electronics, furniture and steel coils, general purpose
containers are used. In approximately 85% of the general purpose shipping containers, the frame
and exterior is made entirely from steel. The remaining containers are made from aluminium
or plywood with ﬁbre-reinforced plastic; in any case the frame is made from steel. The ﬂoor is
usually covered in plywood while the internal walls have different lashing possibilities depending
on the manufacturer. A typical general purpose container can be seen in ﬁgure 2.9. In regards to
requirements on structural integrity, weatherproofness and dimensions, these standard are deﬁned
through the International Convention for Safe containers (CSC) and several ISO-standards .
Relevant parts of these conventions and regulations will be summarised in the following section.
==PAGE:24==
24
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
Figure 2.9: A typical general purpose. Source: ABC Containers
2.4.1
Regulatory framework
Ocean going containers are designed and approved by classiﬁcation societies to ISO-standards
and are subjected to regular inspections on basis of the International Convention for Safe
Containers [IMO, 1996]. The ISO-standards describe the container’s external and internal
dimensions, what loads they need to withstand and criteria for assessing weatherproofness. The
CSC covers regulations on construction, inspection, approval and certiﬁcation of containers. The
regulations herein need to be meet for issuance of the CSC approval plate, seen in ﬁgure 2.10.
==PAGE:25==
2.4 Intermodal Shipping Container Fire Safety
25
Figure 2.10: A typical approval plate found on shipping containers, Source: ABC Containers
The International Convention for Safe Containers (CSC)
The International Convention for Safe Containers entered into force on the 6th of September 1977
after a rapid increase in the use of freight containers. The aim of the convention was and still is to
ensure: ’...a high level of safety of human life in the transport and handling of containers by pro-
viding generally acceptable test procedures and related strength requirements.’ and ’...facilitate
the international transport of containers by providing uniform international safety regulations,
equally applicable to all modes of surface transport.’[IMO, 2017]. In regards to safety of human
lives the convention covers the structural test procedures that the lifting corners, frame, sides and
doors need to live up to, in order to get approved for service[IMO, 1996]. The typical parts of a
general purpose shipping container and areas often experiencing issues can be seen in ﬁgure 2.11.
The load bearing capacity of the container is visible from the CSC approval plate, seen in
ﬁgure 2.10. The stackability of the container is indicated via the ’Allowable Stackable Weight at
1.8 g’. This is the mass of the containers that can be placed on top of the container in question.
The 1.8 originates from the additional g-forces exerted on the containers from a ship’s motions
in heavy seas. This factor is dictated by the CSC and for a container to receive the CSC safety
plate it must be tested that it can support its own mass and the indicated mass multiplied by 1.8.
Currently ordinary 40 ft. shipping containers are able to support the weight of 8 other containers
stacked on top of them.
The CSC requires container to be periodically inspected for faults or approval of modiﬁca-
tions and repairs. There are two ways of ensuring that the containers follow the CSC guidelines.
==PAGE:26==
26
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
One being that there is an ’Approved Continuous Examination Programme (ACEP)’, the second
being a ’Periodic Examination Scheme’. If the later is chosen, dates clearly indication last and
next inspection date must be visible on CSC approval plate, as seen in the right corner of ﬁgure
2.10. If an ACEP is in place, this is indicated on the approval plate as well, with the letters ACEP.
In both cases the container must be inspected every 30 months, to ensure that it is ﬁt for purpose
and comply with CSC.
Figure 2.11: A diagram explaining the different parts of a container and a description of areas
typically experiencing problems. Source: Lloyd’s Register
ISO-standards
For general purpose cargo containers the the following ISO-standards are applied by classiﬁcation
societies to ensure uniform dimensions and test procedures.
• ISO 668: Classiﬁcation Dimensions and Ratings
• ISO 1161: Corner Fittings, Speciﬁcation
• ISO 1496-1: Speciﬁcation and Testing. Part 1: General Cargo Containers for General
Purposes
The ISO-Standard ISO 668 deﬁnes the dimensions that the container has to comply with,
both internally and externally. It also deﬁnes a container’s load-bearing capacity, i.e. ratings R.
All ISO-containers have corner ﬁttings located at the end of the container’s frame elements. The
dimensions and locations of these cast iron elements, seen in ﬁgure 2.12, are described in ISO
1161. ISO 1496-1 describe the test procedures that need to be applied to evaluate whether the
container complies with the ratings described in ISO 668. These are very much in line with the
test procedures described by CSC. An example of the test procedures for investigating stacking
capabilities of the container, require that it is loaded to its rating multiplied by 1.8, i.e. R×1.8.
Further all four corners are loaded with 3767kN simultaneously, to simulate an superimposed
mass of 8 containers at 1.8g, i.e. a mass of 213,360kg at 1.0g.
==PAGE:27==
2.4 Intermodal Shipping Container Fire Safety
27
Figure 2.12: A picture of the a corner ﬁtting, that are ﬁtted on each corner of all ISO/Containers.
These allow for lifting and fastening of containers, Source: Lloyd’s Register
2.4.2
Safety implications
Due to the design of a general purpose container it is generally difﬁcult to detect a ﬁres ignited
within it, by sight. The casing of the container restricts the view to the interior, but the con-
tainer itself may also prove beneﬁcial, from an ﬁre preventive point of view. Relevant safety
implications of the container design and use are covered in the following sections.
Oxygen supply
A ﬁre needs three elements to originate, oxygen, heat and fuel. By removing one of these
elements, the combustive reaction will cease, thereby extinguishing the ﬁre. A sealed 20ft
container, assuming no leakage, can approximately contain 33 m3 of air, as presented by ﬁgure
2.13.
Figure 2.13: A general purpose container’s dimensions
==PAGE:28==
28
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
By loading the container, much of this air will be replaced by the volume of the cargo. The
combustion inside a sealed container should therefore be limited to the amount of available
oxygen and terminate prior to being able to affect adjacent containers. Most intermodal shipping
containers are however equipped with a series of small passive air vents. These are applied for
air pressure equalization, see ﬁgure 2.14.
Figure 2.14: Standard ISO-vent. Source: Billie Box
This is mainly to prevent sudden changes in relative internal pressure of the containers, which
could be caused by the climatic changes that the containers are exposed to during voyage and
further to prohibit condensation inside the containers. The limited amount of ventilated air may
in some cases be enough to oxidize the igniting ﬁre, allowing it to smolder or progress.
Environmental factors
It is quite limited what the professionals working with shipping actually know about the individual
shipping container. The research on the general shipping container’s life is quite limited. Some
general research can however be summarized. The humid and salty environment at sea is tough
on the shipping container. The life-expectancy is 10-15 years depending on how intensively
it was used, i.e. how much of the container’s "life" is spent in the hostile environment at sea
and in tropical regions. From a study conducted in 2000 on optimisation and management of
container usage, an average usage-proﬁle of containers was derived, [Crinks, 2000]. This has
been presented in ﬁgure 2.15. The study showed that the majority of the container’s life-span
was spent idle at storage facilities and only 16% of the time at sea. Both of these are relatively
controlled environments with limited human interaction; of course spill of liquids and solids
inside the container may happen during transit that may inﬂuence the life-span of the container.
6% of the time, the containers are used on land, which may be a very open environment where a
lot of variables are present both physical, chemical and environmental.
==PAGE:29==
2.4 Intermodal Shipping Container Fire Safety
29
Figure 2.15: The usage-proﬁle for a shipping container Crinks, P. (2000) Container Usage Asset
Management in the Global Container Logistics Chain, International Asset Systems.
After a serious ﬁre aboard "Eugene Maersk" the DMAIB decided to investigate whether the
state of or previous use of the container could have been a contributing factor in starting the
ﬁre[DMAIB, 2014]. The investigation started at Maersk, i.e. the ship owner. As they had no
knowledge of the state of the container or of its origins except for the pick-up location; they
referred them to the freight forwarding company Blue Water Shipping. From here they were put
in contact with a London-based broker, who put them in contact with a Chinese broker. In the
end the puzzle was never solved to a degree where the DMAIB could pin-point the place where
the container was packed or state of the container when shipped, see appendix A.1. This meant
that it was not possible to evaluate whether the location for packing the container might have
been a contributing factor in starting the ﬁre. Considering the environmental factors described
above and the Eugene Maersk case, one might consider the inspection interval of 30 months
another possible route of safety implications.
Extinguishing difﬁculties
As described in section 2.4.1, an ISO shipping container is a standardized construction, mainly
manufactured from steel. The container is designed to withstand various types of shipment,
shifting loads, rough storage and handling in order to secure safe and efﬁcient transport of
numerous types of goods within the containerized intermodal freight system. Due to the
containers high structural strength and the way it is designed to seal and secure the interior
cargo, the container may also form a barrier which may prevent effective application of available
ﬁreﬁghting means, i.e. carbon dioxide(CO2) and water. Considering extinguishing with CO2,
all available oxygen inside the container should be displaced for it to be entirely effective. To
achieve this, CO2 would have to enter through the ISO-vents described earlier. Considering
extinguishing with water, the best case scenario would allow for water to be administered directly
to the burning material inside the container. To achieve this, the crew has container piercing
water mist spears available on the ship that depending on design may be accompanied by a hole
==PAGE:30==
30
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
saw to penetrate the front of the container. If this proves ineffective the crew is left with only
being able to cool the outside of and surrounding containers.
2.5
Fire ﬁghting arrangements
Safety of Life at Sea(SOLAS) chapter II-2 describes the current international regulations on ﬁre
safety aboard ships. The regulation are split into several sections dealing with its speciﬁc subject.
Below regulations relevant to suppression of ﬁre aboard container ships will be presented. The
following subjects of SOLAS are covered in the section2.5.1:
• Water quantities, pressure, hose lengths
• CO2 quantities
• Personal protective equipment required for ﬁre ﬁghting
• Regulations imposed on ships carrying IMDG
• Recent amendments to SOLAS, implemented to increase the crew’s ﬁre ﬁghting effective-
ness
2.5.1
Regulatory framework
Some efforts have recently been made in this area to provide crews on large container ships with
better equipment to ﬁght cargo ﬁres. The regulations were updated in 2014 and set into effect for
new ships the 1st of January 2016 [MEPC, 2014]. The general regulations will be summarized
below.
Water delivery
The primary means of extinguishing ﬁres aboard ships is water. Water is of course readily
available around the ship, but pumps and pipes are needed to distribute it to required areas. In
relation to the distribution of water the diameter of the ﬁre mains need to be sufﬁciently large
to transport 140m3/h. The ﬁre mains distribute the water to the hydrants around the ship. A
sufﬁcient number of ﬁre hydrants should be located around the ship so it is possible to reach any
part of the ship, while there is no cargo. The pressure at the hydrants should at least be 0.27
N/mm2 for cargo ships of 6000 gross tonnage(GT) and upwards. For ships smaller than 6000
gross tonnage the pressure is required be at least 0.25N/mm2 at the hydrant. In any case the
maximum pressure should not be larger than a sailor can handle it. The pumping capacity of
the sanitary, bilge, ballast water pumps or other service pumps may be utilized as ﬁre pumps.
They may however not on regular basis have been used for pumping oil or fuel oil. Cargo ships
larger than 1000 GT must have at least 2 ﬁre pumps. The space containing the ﬁre pump can not
be situated adjacent to Machinery space Class-A or rooms containing the "main" ﬁre pump. If
this is not practical, the boundary bulkheads should be insulated to the same speciﬁcations as a
control station, i.e. A-60. Total capacity of the required ﬁre pumps shall be able to deliver the
previously speciﬁed pressure. Each of the required ﬁre pumps, other than the emergency pump,
shall have a capacity not less than 80% of the total required capacity divided by the minimum
number of required ﬁre pumps but in any case not less than 25 m3
h and each such pump shall in
any event be capable of delivering at least two jets of water. These ﬁre pumps shall be capable
of supplying the ﬁre main system under the required conditions. Where more pumps than the
minimum of required pumps are installed such additional pumps shall have a capacity of at least
25 m3
h and shall be capable of delivering at least the two jets of water. In terms of hoses there
shall be a ﬁre hose per 30 meters of ship and 1 spare. For ships carrying dangerous goods 3
additional hoses and nozzles must be available. On ships, with a breadth exceeding 30 m, the
hoses on deck must be between 10 and 25 meters.
==PAGE:31==
2.5 Fire ﬁghting arrangements
31
Carbon dioxide ﬂooding system
For cargo ships 2000 GT and above, the CO2 ﬁre extinguishing system must be ﬁtted, so that
it can suppress ﬁre in cargo spaces. The amount of CO2 that needs to be carried depends on
which gross volume is the largest 30% of the cargo space, 40% of machinery spaces excluding
the engine casing or 35% of the machinery spaces including the engine casing. The calculated
largest gross volume then equals to the volume of CO2, which must to be carried.
Carriage of dangerous goods
The deﬁnitions in SOLAS are quite determined in this area. If a shipowner wishes to transport
dangerous goods in amounts greater than a few kilos or litres the ship must comply with the
provisions of SOLAS II-2 Regulation 19 [SOLAS, 2009]. The requirements herein do not
necessarily constitute harsher regulations than stated in other relevant part of SOLAS. On top of
the SOLAS regulations much of the regulatory framework required to comply is stated in the
IMDG Code, which will be presented later on. To summarize the provisions the following areas
are relevant:
• water supplies, which mostly dictates the amount of water that the ﬁre suppression system
needs to be able to deliver and how much water the bilge pumps are required to able to
remove from the ship;
• the remaining provisions deal with personal protection equipment and the availability of
portable ﬁre extinguishers.
A reference table of the regulations that apply to container ships is presented, for the convenience
of the SOLAS users at the end of regulation 19.
To ensure water supplies for ﬁre ﬁghting, arrangements must be made so it is readily avail-
able. Readily available meaning that they either have to be pressurised at all times or is remote
controllable. In addition to the 2 nozzles required in earlier regulations 2 more must be present,
the same rules on pressure and water ﬂow apply. To cool the surrounding areas in a below-deck
ﬁre, arrangements have to be in place to provide at least 5 liters of water per square meter
horizontal surfaces in the cargo space per minute. Achieving this is possible through ﬁxed spray-
ing nozzles or by ﬂooding the cargo space with water. Further, personal protection equipment
must be available to shield the sailors against chemicals. The ship needs to carry 4 complete
set of clothing resistant to chemical agents, besides the two ﬁre-ﬁghting suits. Also 2 extra
self-contained breathing apparatus(SCBA) are required in addition too the two already required.
Recent efforts
Per the 1st of January 2016 new amendments to SOLAS came into effect [MEPC, 2014]. This
included updates on what ﬁre suppression and extinguishing systems need to be installed aboard
container ships and vessels in general.
The 2 major changes, dealing with container ﬁres can be seen in ﬁgure 2.16 and 2.17. The
new rules dictate that a container ship must carry at least 1 water mist lance, the ﬁrst piece of
equipment presented. Also the ship must carry 2 portable water monitors if it is below 30 m in
breadth or 4 if it is above.
==PAGE:32==
32
Chapter 2. A Perspective on Container Ship Fires
Figure 2.16: Example of a water mist lance as made necessary by MSC.365(93), Source: Huayan
As for the water mist lance, the only requirements are that it is able to penetrate the wall of
a container and that it is readily available to the crew. For the portable water monitors, the
requirement is that their water beam must be able to reach the top tiers of containers and the
additional water consumption must accounted for in the total pumping capacity.
Figure 2.17: Example of a portable water monitor as made necessary by MSC.365(93),
Source:Huayan
2.5.2
Safety implications
With the development of the ULCSs, a rising unease within the maritime industry have been
seen concerning the ship and crew’s ability to extinguish cargo ﬁres. As section 3 on hazard
identiﬁcation will shed light on, the crew is often placed in a difﬁcult position when faced
with cargo ﬁres. The new tools, i.e. the water mist spears and water monitors are deﬁnitely
a step in the right direction in terms of being able to ﬁght ﬁre inside containers, but ﬁres that
the ship crews are unable to tackle independently are still occurring, see Tab.1.1. The reason
may be that the ﬁre suppression technologies on board the container ships have not followed
the same innovative progress/evolution as the ship itself, as seen from interview with Øssur
==PAGE:33==
2.5 Fire ﬁghting arrangements
33
Jarleivson Hilduberg, Head of the Danish Maritime Investigation Board, see appendix A.1. The
ﬁre protection arrangements currently applied on large container ships, are fundamentally the
same applied decades ago for much smaller ships. The quantity of delivered water has increased
and the quantity of CO2 available has increased, but in the end success or failure of the ﬁre
ﬁghting efforts rests on the crew being able and willing to enter e.g. a cargo space below deck to
apply water in the container that is on ﬁre. Manned hoses are also still the main resource for
combating ﬁres above the weather deck of the ships. Although, as the water monitors become
more widely implemented, this may perhaps become more favored.
Inert gas ﬁre extinguishing is for most cases seen as the last resort in combating ﬁre. De-
ploying CO2 and previously halon can be an effective means of extinguishing a ﬁre in a cargo
space or engine room as it displaces oxygen, which in turn may smother a ﬁre. Dependant on
how air-tight the cargo space is, the concentrations of CO2 will remain reasonably high for some
time. Unfortunately the temperature in the cargo space is not lowered signiﬁcantly by the CO2’s
presence. This means that when oxygen is introduced into the cargo space again the ﬁre may
ﬂare up if the previously burning materials is still above its auto-ignition temperature. Further
CO2 in concentrations above 9% will cause humans to go unconsciousness within minutes. If
the SCBA has any leaks, sending people into the cargo space again to deal with such a ﬂare up
could prove fatal.
==PAGE:34==
3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Containerized cargo impose a series of hazards to the container ship, its crew and the environment.
A hazard is by the authors understood as a source or situation with a potential to harm these. The
purpose of the following sections will be to investigate the hazards of certain types of cargoes,
by initially determining which cargo types historically have caused ﬁres. From the documented
historical cargo ﬁres, in-depth research will be conducted on some of the most frequent causes
of these. This includes presenting the narrative from select well-documented cases concerning
ﬁres caused by relevant cargo types. The research further includes identifying the potential
hazards, i.e. hazard identiﬁcation of these cargo types. The scope of this investigation will not
be to uncover usual work place hazards like dropped objects, moving parts or in general hazards
located in the accommodation space. Hazard imposed by the cargo on the crew, as a result of a
cargo ﬁre will to some extent be presented.
3.1
Fire scenarios
To investigate the hazard concerning container ﬁres the European Maritime Safety Agency’s
(EMSA) register of maritime casualties was investigated for relevant accident reports. EMSA
joins the individual EU-Countries maritime investigatory board’s reports into a single searchable
database. The Danish Maritime Accident Investigation Board (DMAIB), is the sole organisation
responsible for investigating maritime accidents involving Danish ﬂagged ships. DMAIB
is also an excellent source of maritime accident reports as they are required to make these
available to the public. This also applies to the German investigatory agency; Federal Bureau
of Maritime Casualty Investigation (BSU) and the French marine casualties investigation board
called BEAmer. Further a few less in depth documented cases have been included. These have
been found via maritime news articles describing maritime accidents. However these typically
include statements from port authorities, coast guards or ﬁreﬁghting personal at the scene. An
overview of documented cases relevant to this thesis are presented in Table 3.1, and categorized
based on year, ship and the cargo which was found to have initiated the ﬁre.

Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
In the following chapter these accidents will be used to classify the substances or articles
which are identiﬁed by the authors of this thesis, as the main hazards to the ﬁre safety aboard the
container ships. Further the identiﬁed hazards and the related ﬁre scenarios will be applied in a
cause consequence study to investigate patterns among the causes and derive general frequencies
of escalation path. For the time being this section should serve as an introduction to well-known
cargo container ﬁres aboard container ships and their apparent causes.
To give the reader an introduction to these ﬁre scenarios and insight into the severity of these ﬁres,
a selected few will be elaborated below. A couple of obvious causes repeat over the years, as
can be seen in table 3.1, which are the shipment of calcium hypochlorite, charcoal and batteries.
Therefore three ﬁre incidents involving the shipment of these cargoes will be elaborated below.
Further an introduction to the ﬁre incident that took place on MSC Flamnia in 2012, will be
presented, due to the severity of this incident.
3.1.1
2003 - MV Sea Elegance - Calcium hypochlorite
All of the below information was attained from the South African Maritime Safety Authority
marine accident report about the ﬁre [Authority, 2005].
Table 3.2: Short summary - Fire scenario - MV Sea Elegance
Ship:
MV Sea Elegance
Capacity:
766 TEU
Ship location:
Anchored of Durban, South Africa
Igniting material:
Calcium Hypochlorite
Initiation date:
11th October 2003
Termination date:
18th October 2003
Fire duration:
8 days
Origin of ﬁre/container position:
Cargo hold
Consequences:
Loss of life, Damage to containers, damage to reefer cargo and
damage to ship (CO2 room)
Return date to service of ship:
Unclear
MV Sea Elegance is a 171 m long container ship, sailing under the Singaporean ﬂag.
On October 11th 2003, during voyage from Port Louis (Republic of Mauritius) to Durban
(South Africa), an explosion occurred. It originated from cargo hold number 6, just aft of the
accommodation. Further, a ﬁre was discovered in cargo hold 9 located close to amidships.
Fire detection and initial steps
The explosion and the following ﬁre was detected at 07:55, when an explosion occurred just
aft of the accommodation in cargo hold 6. After the initial explosion a series of ﬁreballs shot
out from cargo hold. Smoke dispersed into the accommodation area and engine room, ﬁlling
it rapidly. The master sounded the general alarm and the 3rd mate and an able seaman(AB)
reported to the bridge where they were instructed to muster the crew. As the remaining crew were
having breakfast in the galley the 3rd mate went there to pick up the crew and take them to the
mustering station. This was made difﬁcult by smoke at the exits, which caused the crew having
to escape from the portholes in the galley. After escaping the accommodation area the crew
mustered on the deck, where it was concluded that one crew was missing. Due to the severity of
the situation the master instructed the 3rd mate and AB to start lowering the starboard lifeboat, as
the lifeboat located on the port side was engulfed in ﬂames. Further, the radio equipment on the
bridge was destroyed by the ﬁre which made it impossible for the master to submit a distress call.
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Fire scenarios
37
Ships anchored in vicinity of the MV Sea Elegance called in the ﬁre to the port, which made
them dispatch 2 harbor tugs for ﬁreﬁghting.
Fire suppression
The tugs arrived 30 minutes after the distress call and shortly after these tugs were joined by two
more harbor tugs, a salvage tug and a sea rescue craft. Crew from the Durban ﬁre brigade were
transferred to the vessel and extinguished the ﬁre in the accommodation, but didn’t manage to
extinguish the ﬁre in the containers. Members of the rescue team boarded the ship and initiated a
search for the missing sailor, their efforts did not pay of. As the engine room had been made
inaccessible by the ﬁre, control of the ﬁre pumps weren’t possible from there. The international
shore connection was therefore used for delivering water to the ﬁreﬁghting efforts. Even with
the external ﬁreﬁghting support the ﬁre was not extinguished till the ship was brought into port
on the 18th of October.
Origin of the ﬁre
The ﬁre supposedly originated from a container stored below deck containing 20 t of calcium
hypochlorite, UN 1748. The cargo had been stored below deck, as it hadn’t been declared a
dangerous good. Remaining containers in the cargo hold carried plastic, rubber and paper goods.
Ambient temperatures in cargo hold were 35oC on average and the container itself, but had been
placed up against the HFO storage tanks, which also provides at source of heat. As calcium
hypochlorite becomes unstable at temperatures exceeding 35oC when carried in such quantities,
the experts agreed that it was the cause of the explosion and subsequent ﬁre.
3.1.2
2015 - CAROLINE MAERSK - Charcoal
All of the below information is attained from the DMAIB Marine accident report, issued in April
2016 [DMAIB, 2016].
Table 3.3: Short summary - Fire scenario - CAROLINE MAERSK
Ship:
CAROLINE MAERSK
Capacity:
8660 TEU
Ship location:
South China Sea
Igniting material:
Charcoal
Initiation date:
26 August 2015
Termination date:
30 August 2015
Fire duration:
4 days
Origin of ﬁre/container position:
Cargo hold
Consequences:
Damage to containers, damage to reefer cargo and
damage to ship (CO2 room)
Return date to service of ship:
6 September 2015
Caroline MAERSK is a 347 m long container ship, sailing under the danish ﬂag. The ship crew
comprises a total of 22 persons, with varying range of nationalities. On August 26th 2015, during
voyage from Chiwan (China) to Tanjung Pelepas (Malaysia), approximately 50 nm from the
coast of Vietnam, a ﬁre was discovered in cargo hold 9 positioned close to the midships section
of the ship.
Fire detection
The ﬁre was detected through a smoke alarm signal which indicated smoke in the respective hold.
The signal was conﬁrmed by crew members working on deck, who reported smoke emerging
from the hatch cover near the area of the signal. As initial response the ship speed was reduced
==PAGE:38==
38
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
and the course of the ship was altered to re-direct the smoke from the deck. The ventilation to
the hold was terminated and all electrical power switched off.
The ﬁrst crew response was initiated approximately 16 minutes after the signaling of the ﬁre.
At this point two two-man teams had been briefed and equipped with ﬁreﬁghting outﬁts, SCBA
and ﬁre hoses. To determine the location and extent of the ﬁre, the two two-man teams were
sent into the hold in shifts. The ﬁrst team struggled to enter and maneuver within the hold,
due to the pressurized ﬁre hose and the limited visibility caused by the heavy smoke. After 20
minutes the ﬁrst team returned to the weather deck as their air supply was running low. They
had not succeeded in identifying the source of the ﬁre. The second team was able to identify the
container which they considered to be the source of the smoke, located three or four levels down
at the starboard side of the hold. The crew had still no idea about the severity of the ﬁre, extent
or how many containers were affected.
Fire suppression
It had been decided to proceed at full speed towards the original port of call, Tanjung Pelepas.
During the four day voyage a number of difﬁculties in the ﬁreﬁghting response was experienced.
During the ﬁrst day, in parallel with the detection teams response in the hold, initial ﬁre suppres-
sion response was carried out by the crew on deck, consisting of boundary cooling of adjacent
areas.
Based on the information provided by the two detection teams, it was decided to deploy the CO2
extinguishing system. As a standard procedure the whole crew was mustered on the open deck
outside, at the starboard side of the A-deck, above the CO2-room. At the point of the release of
the CO2, the door to the CO2 room was blown open followed by a big white cloud. The outburst
was caused, as the main distribution valve for the cargo holds was closed, which had resulted in
the burst of a gasket on the main pipeline. A second section of CO2 cylinders was subsequently
released, which was considered to have reached the hold even though CO2 was still leaking out
of the room.
In the evening during preparation to enter the hold to initiate a manual ﬁre ﬁghting response, the
compressor for ﬁlling the breathing apparatus bottles broke down. At the point of the compressor
returning into working order the ﬁrst crew entered the hold, where they discovered that the
burning container was severely blackened and unidentiﬁable. The second ﬁre response team was
equipped with the ship’s special container ﬁreﬁghting equipment. The equipment consisted of a
cordless power drill, a hole saw bit and a spike nozzle. In the attempt to extinguish the ﬁre within
the container, the team tried to drill a hole in the container casing which would allow them to
penetrate the casing with the spike nozzle and thereby spray water directly into the container.
Unfortunately the drill proved ineffective by not being able to penetrate the container casing.
The crew was therefore forced to return to the deck in order to reevaluate the ﬁre suppression plan.
On the second day a second attempt to penetrate the container casing was initiated. This
time the crew applied an angle grinder, by which they managed to cut the hole and apply water
within the container. The ﬁre was soon after considered under control.
But later the same day an increasing amount of smoke was detected from the hold. An investiga-
tion team discovered a temperature increase in the containers adjacent to the one where the ﬁre
initially originated from. In order to keep the ﬁre under control it was, after some time decided
to cut holes in the adjacent containers as well in order to ﬂood them with water. As only one
special container spike nozzle was available, the crew was forced to apply standard ﬁre nozzle in
the two adjacent containers.
==PAGE:39==
3.1 Fire scenarios
39
On day three the crew discovered elevated temperatures and smoke coming from hold 8, which
is placed adjacent to cargo hold 9 and separated by an open bulkhead. During investigations
the crew found smoldering charcoal spread around the decks in the hold, which had caused the
temperature of the containers closest to the smoldering charcoal to rise to approximately 80oC.
Through a ﬁre suppression response lasting approximately 1 hour and 30 min, where the crew
applied ﬁre hoses, they were able to extinguish the coal. The crew subsequently decided to
request assistance from an external professional ﬁreﬁghting team prior to arriving at the Tanjung
Pelepas container terminal.
On day four the requested salvage crew was immediately put in charge of the ﬁre suppres-
sion response on the ship, prior arriving at the port. After the ship arrived at Tanjung Pelepas,
the still burning containers was off-loaded to the shore.
Two days after the container ship arrived at the port, the ship could proceed to Singapore
for repairs.
Origin of the ﬁre
The cargo which initiated the container ﬁre on Caroline MAERSK was later identiﬁed as shisha
charcoal. The container had been shipped from a manufacturer in China and loaded on to the ship
in Busan (South Korea) 10 days before the ﬁre broke out. The cause of ignition was allegedly
due to self-heating of the charcoal. An elaboration on the chemical reaction which can cause the
self-heating of charcoal, will be presented in section 3.2.
The container manifest provided by the shipper of the cargo, described the content of the
container as "tablet for water pipe". But the cargo had not been declared as dangerous goods,
even though the IMDG code states that charcoal is a Class 4.2 cargo, which covers substances
liable to spontaneous combustion.
3.1.3
2016 - CMA CGM ROSSINI - Lithium-ion batteries
All of the below information is attained from the BEAmer Marine accident report, issued in
April 2016 [BEAmer, 2017].
Table 3.4: Short summary - Fire scenario - CMA CGM ROSSINI
Ship:
CMA CGM ROSSINI
Capacity:
5770 TEU
Ship location:
Colombo, at port
Igniting material:
Lithium-ion batteries
Initiation date:
15th June 2016
Termination date:
19th June 2016
Fire duration:
5 days
Origin of ﬁre/container position:
Cargo hold
Consequences:
Damage to containers, damage to other cargo and
damage to ship
Return date to service of ship:
17th July 2016
CMA CGM ROSSINI is a 277 m long container ship, sailing under the French ﬂag. The
ship’s crew comprises a total of 29 persons, with undeﬁned nationalities. The explosion and
subsequent ﬁre occurred on August 15th 2016, while the ship was performing cargo operations in
==PAGE:40==
40
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
the Port of Colombo in Sri Lanka. From the report the implicated containers were two ordinary
40 ft general purpose cargo containers, weighting 16.692 and 17.417 t. The containers carried 26
and 25 pallets with 104 and 100 drums of lithium-ion batteries.
Fire detection and initial steps
An indications of the ﬁre was initially discovered by crew members securing lashings around
cargo hold 5(location of the ﬁre) at 09:20. Before they had the chance to inform the ofﬁcer on
watch(OOW), an explosion occurred. Subsequently the crew informed the OOW immediately.
Via internal UHF radio the remaining crew was alerted at 09:30, which caused the chief engineer
to turn off the ventilation for all holds and also informing the master. Between 09:35 and 09:38
the general alarm was sounded, the crew ran hoses either side and the main ﬁre-pump was started.
Further the port was informed and the ﬁreﬁghters were equipped.
Fire suppression
From 09:39 water was sprayed through the hatch covers with two ﬁre-hoses, which caused
thick smoke to rise out of the hold. The applied water produced an alarm concerning high
water level in the bilge well at 09:49. At 09:52 a smoke alarm was triggered concerning the
affected cargo space. Fireﬁghting crew from the port arrived at 09:54 and advised the crew to
commence applying water. An additional smoke detection alarm was produced for the cargo
hold at 10:15. The water based ﬁreﬁghting means were utilized until 12:46, when the equipment
was withdrawn to allow for complete shut-down of the cargo space in preparation for discharge
of CO2. Discharge was commenced at 13:21, also water was sprayed on the hatch covers in
efforts to cool these. Concluding the discharge at 14:35 326 bottles of CO2 had been used,
which provided a general temperature decrease of the cargo hold by 16:35. By 20:00 it was
concluded that the ﬁre had reignited, as measurements along the cargo space showed increasing
temperatures. Thursday the 16th June several containers were unloaded to allow for a watertight
gasket to be placed between the deck and the hatch covers, to hinder inﬂow of air to the ongoing
ﬁre. Further, in an effort to combat the ﬁre more efﬁciently an high expansion ﬁre extinguishing
foam was applied to the cargo hold through an air vent at 09:40. As supply of high expansion
foam ran short during the operation, less effective low expansion foam was applied, which ended
at 20:20. The operation was considered ineffective and it was decided to ﬂood cargo hold 5 with
10 m of water, however this approach was abandoned as the temperatures in the cargo hold had
dropped to satisfaction on the following day. The following days temperatures around the hold
were continuously monitored till the 19th June, when a ﬁreﬁghting team went on an expedition
into the hold and declared the ﬁre extinguished.
Origin and cause of the ﬁre
It was established that the ﬁre originated from one of two containers carrying lithium-ion batteries
destined for recycling. As the ambient temperatures were about 32oC, it was assumed that this
wasn’t the cause of the ﬁre. Alternative causes were considered, the most likely being a short-
circuit of the positive and negative terminal of a battery. This would have cased a runaway
reaction and subsequent igniting of the battery. However, the batteries had prior to shipping
received individual pieces of polyethylene adhesive tape covering the terminals to mitigate this
issue. The batteries were packed in the same fashion as seen in Fig.3.1. Whether the tape failed
or wasn’t there in the ﬁrst place couldn’t be established, as any evidence had been lost in the ﬁre.
The shipper proposed that rough handling of the shipment might have been a factor in causing
this. BEAmer points out that such cargo should preferably be packed with sand in between the
layers of batteries, to act as an electrically insulating layer.
==PAGE:41==
3.1 Fire scenarios
41
Figure 3.1: Picture showing how the batteries in question were packed. Source: BEAmer
3.1.4
2012 - MSC FLAMINIA - Divinylbenzene
All of the below information is attained from the DSU Marine accident report, issued in April
2016 [BSU, 2014].
Table 3.5: Short summary - Fire scenario - CAROLINE MAERSK
Ship:
MSC FLAMINIA
Capacity:
6750 TEU
Ship location:
The North Atlantic Ocean
Igniting material:
Divinylbenzene

approx. 21 August 2015
