Cargo hold
Consequences:
Loss of lives, human casualties, Damage to containers,
damage to reefer cargo
Ship returned to service:
July 2014
MSC FLAMINIA is a 300 m long container ship, which is sailing under German ﬂag.
During the ﬁre and subsequent explosion, the ship’s population counted 23 crew members and 2
passengers traveling to Europe. The ﬁre occurred during a voyage from Charleston in the United
States to Antwerp in Belgium, more speciﬁcally on open sea in the North Atlantic Ocean at
coordinates 48o13.8′N 027o57.9′W. As consequence of the above three crew lost their lives, two
were severely injured, the ship and cargo suffered extensive damage.
Fire detection and initial steps
Smoke was detected on the 14th of July at 05:42:18 in the morning. The sample extraction smoke
detection system on the bridge indicated presence of smoke in cargo hold 4. At that time only to
two crew had shifts, this included the chief ofﬁcer(CO) and an able bodied seaman. To assess the
situation the seaman was tasked by the CO to investigate the what had caused the alarm. Upon
inspection the seaman conﬁrmed to the CO via his portable radio that smoke was coming from
==PAGE:42==
42
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
the cargo hold. At 5:00 the ﬁre alarm was sound to alert the off duty crew of the situation. To
let the crew understand the seriousness of the situation, the internal communication system was
used for informing the crew that it wasn’t a drill. The crew and passengers were accounted for at
the mustering stations. To protect the mustering station on the A-deck of combustion gasses the
ship’s course was altered, so that these would be blown of the port side of the ship.
Fire suppression
Initially the ﬁre was suppressed by shutting down and closing of all ventilation to cargo hold
4, i.e. establishing a closed-down state. Subsequently the team responsible for this gathered at
the safety store in which the ﬁre-ﬁghting equipment was stored. Meanwhile the part of the crew
tasked performing the ﬁre-ﬁghting operation had put on their equipment.
The main engine was stopped at 06:21, further the air conditioning and all ventilation shut
off at the same time.
To extinguish/suppress the ﬁre it was decided to discharge CO2 into the affected cargo hold.
The discharge started at 06:42, meanwhile the CO2 alarm for the engine room started causing the
auxiliary boilers and fan for the main engine to stop automatically. This wasn’t intentional and
the technical ofﬁcers were unable to ﬁnd the cause of this. The technical ofﬁcers were tasked
with getting the systems and main engine up and running again. This left them unable to attend
the ﬁre ﬁghting efforts on the deck. CO2 was discharged again to suppress the ﬁre in cargo hold
4.
To cool down the area around cargo hold 4 seven crew members were tasked with preparing
hoses to supply water for the affected area. At 08:04, during this operation a heavy explosion
occurred in the area of cargo space 4. The explosion caused injuries and critical injuries to four
of the crew members, also one crew member had gone missing. Further, the explosion caused
several containers placed on the deck to fall aboard and also onto the passages on either side of
the ship, effectively cutting of the crew forward of the explosion from the rest of the ship. Due to
the severity of the situation the ship’s command choose to abandon ship and mayday was called
via VHF at 08:27.
Abandoning the ship
The crew in the aft section of the ship initially occupied the port side lifeboat. This plan was
abandoned due to several containers ﬂoating in the area where it was supposed to enter the water.
The starboard side lifeboat was therefore put into use and sailed to the fore part of the ship,
where the remaining crew had been caught earlier. A pilot ladder was lowered to the lifeboat
and the fore located life raft was deployed beside the lifeboat and attached to it. The uninjured
and slightly injured used the ladder to get aboard the lifeboat, while the critically injured crew
member was lowered into the life raft and tended too. The lifeboat was sailed away from the ship
with the life raft being towed, behind it. At 10:48 a tanker that had picked up the mayday sent
out earlier reach the lifeboat, and achieved to bring all crew members aboard the ship by 12:08.
The aftermath
The aftermath of the ﬁre and subsequent explosion aboard MSC FLAMINIA was quite unusual,
as the salvage operation wasn’t concluded till the 9th of September when the ship was allowed
to enter Wilhelmshaven. The long time between the crew abandoning the ship and the ship being
towed to a port was do to the environmental threat it posed. Several ports were approached in
relation to taking in the ship, but refused to do so cause of the ship’s state.
Origin of the ﬁre
From the investigation report produced by the BSU there were three competing theories on what
initiated the ﬁre and subsequent explosion. Both concern the various dangerous goods carried
in the cargo hold and on the deck of the ship. However, as the ﬁre was allowed to commence
==PAGE:43==
3.1 Fire scenarios
43
for a period of 8 days or more, establishing the original source of the ﬁre was impossible via
inspection of the wreckage. This may be easier to imagine from the picture presented in Fig. 3.2,
which was captured during the ﬁrst survey after the ship was allowed entrance to Wilhemshaven.
Figure 3.2: A picture of MSC Flaminia overlooking the extensive ﬁre damage from the observa-
tion deck. Source: BSU
The different theories therefore build on eyewitness reports and the cargo manifestos, i.e.
description of the shipping container’s content. Initially a report on the cause of the ﬁre was
produced by a Dr Dana Meissner from the Institute of Safety Engineering/Ship Safety in
Rostock. This report pointed to ﬁve possible scenarios that might have caused the ﬁre. These
include ignition of highly combustible materials, dust explosion, products reacting with ambient
substances and cross-reactions between individual cargo types. The relevant cargoes have been
presented in table 3.6.

The above table 3.6 also serves to show the great variety of dangerous goods loaded aboard
the MSC FLAMINIA. This in combination with the time it was allow burn and stay at sea prior to
the initial survey on the cause of ﬁre made it difﬁcult to draw any certain conclusion on the cause
of the ﬁre. The analysis performed in the report provides three realistic scenarios on the cause of
the ﬁre. The ﬁrst takes basis in the ﬂammable gasses contained within the car care products being
released do to damaged or defective packaging. Due to the low ignition energy required to ignite
these, a spark may have caused detonation of the gases. This may have left a smoldering ﬁre that
could have heated the PVC suspension formolon, producing hydrochloric acid. This could then
have reacted with the cargo containers and hull’s steel to produce explosive hydrogen gas, over
==PAGE:45==
3.1 Fire scenarios
45
several days. This would than have been ignited, producing an explosion and a fully developed
ﬁre. The second hypothesis considered that a tank container carrying dimethylethanolamine had
a leakage causing its contents to vaporize and dissolute. This could have been ignited by itself
or formed condensate on the underside of the hatch covers that could have ignited, causing an
explosion. Alternatively the leaking dimethylethanolamine could have reacted with other cargoes
to produce heat. Especially reaction between dimethylethanolamine and containers carrying
PVC suspension would have produced heat and subsequently large quantities of explosive gasses
other several days. These gasses would then have been ignited to produce an explosion
The third theory was based on analysis performed on deposits in the sample extraction smoke
detection system’s piping. Results of these showed that the deposits contained varying quantities
of divinylbenzene, ethylstyrene, diethylbenzene and other associated aromatic hydrocarbons.
From these deposits an expert acting on behalf of the charterer, Dr. Beeley, theorizes polymeriza-
tion of DVB was the main cause of the ﬁre. If divinylbenzene is stored at higher than anticipated
ambient temperatures, the substances put in place to avoid this may deplete prematurely, causing
exothermic reactions, which will be further explored in Sec. 3.2.5. The three tank containers
contained 18 t of divinylbenzene each, had no means of cooling, were placed in the vicinity of
containers ﬁlled with wood pulp that generate heat during storage and the fuel oil tanks that
produce heat as well. The experts speculate that the leak either produced ﬂammable vapors that
were ignited or produced heat that caused the dimethylethanolamine to react violently while
producing heat. Further, the ineffectiveness of the CO2 ﬁre suppression system is speculated
to be caused by the mix of hot gasses produced by heating of the different cargoes, reacting to
deplete the CO2. Also the difference in densities of the combustible gasses possibly available
in the cargo space, CO2 not being the heaviest, might have meant that all combustibles weren’t
displaced.

46
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
3.2
Hazard Identiﬁcation
To get an idea of the most relevant causes of cargo ﬁres, a diagram summarizing all cargoes
thought to have been the initial cause of ﬁres was drawn up. This has been presented in ﬁgure
3.3.
3.2.1
Hazards
Figure 3.3: Hazard identiﬁcation
From ﬁgure 3.3, it can be seen that the containerized shipment of calcium hypochlorite has
been responsible for 28 % of the discovered container ship ﬁre incidents investigated in this
thesis. Further it can be seen that the cargo which has caused the second highest percentage
of ﬁre incidents aboard container ships was the storage and shipment of charcoal. Together
these two cargo types bares the responsibility of almost 50 % of all the explored ﬁre incidents.
The containerized shipment of batteries has caused 5 % of the ﬁres, thereby constituting the
3rd highest risk source of containerized shipment. Besides the ﬁre incidents where it were not
possible to determine the actual cause of ﬁre, the remaining cargoes have been responsible for an
equal percentage of ﬁre incidents.
Due to the high percentage of ﬁre incidents caused by the transport calcium hypochlorite
==PAGE:47==
3.2 Hazard Identiﬁcation
47
and charcoal these two have been identiﬁed as great hazards to the ﬁre safety aboard container
ships. Further the shipment of batteries and devinylbenzene are considered a great risk due to
the amount of ﬁres, severity and extensive damages these cargoes have inﬂicted on the container
ships and their crew.
Together these four types of cargoes, constitutes the highest risk of ﬁre and are therefore,
by the authors of this thesis, identiﬁed as the main hazards to the ﬁre safety aboard container
ships.
In order to explore the chemical nature of the identiﬁed hazards and their individual hazardous
nature in respect to the ﬁre safety aboard container ships, a chemical study was conducted.
An introduction to each of the hazard studies and the main ﬁndings will be elaborated in the
following sections.
3.2.2
Calcium hypochlorite
Hypochlorite is an ion composed of oxygen and chlorine, it has the chemical formula ClO−. To
form hypochlorites the ion is subjected to anions, which is typically calcium or sodium. This in
turn form calcium hypochlorite and sodium hypochlorite. The processes for forming these salts
proceeds as follows:
2Cl2 +2Ca(OH)2 −→
CaCl2 +Ca(ClO)2 +H2O
(3.1)
Cl2 +2NaOH −→
NaCl +NaClO+H2O
(3.2)
As previous studies of cargo container ﬁres have only shown cases of calcium hypochlorite
related ﬁres, the nature of this chemical will be the only one of the two investigated. On an
industrial scale calcium hypochlorite is produced by gas chlorination of hydrated lime, which
has 2-5% free water content.
Knowledge base
Calcium hypochlorite is slightly unstable at normal ambient temperature (24oC). External effects
can however make it react violently. A broad spectrum of physical and chemical’s effects on
calcium hypochlorite were investigated in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981]. From this study 5 brands
of calcium hypochlorite containing approximately 70% available chlorine and 2 variations of
the brands containing approx. 65% available chlorine were investigated for its contents and
reactiveness to contaminants. The results of these analysis have been included in appendix A.2,
table 1 & 2. To summarise, the different brands contain between 0.5-2.0% chlorate(ClO−
3 ) a
highly potent oxidiser. Further, the content of calcium hypochlorite in the brands labeled as
containing 70% hypochlorite actually have 65-74%. This may vary due to the unstable nature
of calcium hypochlorite, i.e. its self-decomposition properties, which will be explained in the
following paragraphs. The content of volatile matter when the samples were heated to 105oC
varied between 2-11%, some fraction of this may be attributed to evaporation of water as the
samples contain some moisture. A secondary and more recent study [Halliburton, 2002] deals in
greater detail with the self-heating and thermal ignition properties of calcium hypochlorite. In
this thesis experiments were conducted on hydrated calcium hypochlorite with 5.5-16% water
content, which was placed in commercial high density polyethylene(HDPE) drums containing
40 kg and ﬁber drums containing 200 kg. This products has the UN reference number UN2880.
The experiments were conducted in a cargo container equipped with heating elements and in
some cases ventilation, to simulate the climate experience on a container ship. This allowed
the authors of [Halliburton, 2002] to assess the critical ambient temperature for which different
batch sizes produce self-heating and thermal ignition. The chemical composition of the calcium
hypochlorite product used in the study resembles that investigated in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981].
==PAGE:48==
48
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Hazards
There are several hazards associated with transporting and storing calcium hypochlorite. These
include release of toxic gas, oxidizers and the that is has self-heating properties. This allows for
several possible trajectories of undesirable event like ﬁres, explosions and poisoning.
Water
Hydrated and anhydrous hypochlorite products have different self-heating properties. The chemi-
cal reaction happening can be seen in Eq.3.3, this reaction is exothermic. From [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981]
adding 2.4 ml of water to 20 grams of hydrated hypochlorite product showed an increase in
temperature of 9 to 15oC while an anhydrous product showed an increase of 17oC. A secondary
experiment using 100 grams of the most reactive product from the previous experiment and 50
ml of water showed an 46oC increase in temperature.
Ca(OCl)2 +2H2O −→2HOCl +Ca(OH)2
(3.3)
Temperature
As described there is no evidence that normal ambient temperatures (24oC) will make hydrated
or anhydrous calcium hypochlorite products like UN2880 or UN1748 self-heat to a degree that
would cause thermal ignition. This applies when the product is carried in standard commercially
available 40 kg HDPE drums and the 200 kg ﬁber drums [Halliburton, 2002]. The two types of
containers used commercially and in the experiments can be seen in Fig.3.4.
(a) Typical 40L HDPE drum used for transporting
chemical products. Source: www.alibaba.com
(b) Typical 200L ﬁbre drum used for transporting
chemical. The inside of the drum is usually covered
with a HDPE liner. Source: www.alibaba.com
Figure 3.4: Typical packaging used for transporting calcium hypochlorite products
The exothermic chemical reactions governing heat generation in calcium hypochlorite products
vary depending on moisture content and the temperature increase rate that it is subjected to.
Anhydrous calcium hypochlorite products decompose mostly according to the reaction described
in Eq.3.4. Secondarily upto 40% of the calcium hypochlorite undergoes a disproportionation
reaction described in Eq. 3.5.
Ca(OCl)2 −→CaCl2 +O2
(3.4)
3Ca(OCl)2 −→Ca(ClO3)2 +2CaCl2
(3.5)
When heated much of the free chlorine gas generated is produced via the reaction described in
Eq.3.6. The reaction presented requires water to propagate, and is therefore most relevant for
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Hazard Identiﬁcation
49
hydrated calcium hypochlorite.
Ca(OCl)2 +CaCl2 +2H2O −→2Ca(OH)2 +2Cl2
(3.6)
Secondly Eq.3.7 displays the process in which carbon dioxide reacts with calcium hypochlorite
to produce calcium carbonate and free chlorine gas. This reaction commences spontaneously
at normal ambient temperature when calcium hypochlorite is subjected to atmospheric CO2.
Although it should be considered that CO2 is a product of almost any combustion process, why
it may available in case of a ﬁre.
Ca(OCl)2 +CaCl2 +2CO2 −→2CaCO3 +2Cl2
(3.7)
The dominant reaction pathway changes with rate of temperature increase. At temperature
increase rates greater than 15oC/min the reaction pathway displayed in Eq.3.4 is dominant, while
at rates lower than 1oC/min the pathways displayed in Eq.3.4 and 3.5 are equally dominant.
From experiments conducted in [Halliburton, 2002], the CAT at which thermal ignition of
hydrated calcium occurred was concluded to be:
• 60.1oC, when a 40kg HDPE drum was subjected to forced convection in a temperature
controlled oven.
• 55.2oC, when a 40kg HDPE drum was subjected to natural convection in a standard
general purpose shipping container.
• 43.4oC, when a 200kg ﬁber drum was subjected to natural convention in the same environ-
ment as the above case.
From the same study it was also concluded that self-heating and thermal ignition experiments
conducted in small-scale(small quantities of calcium hypochlorite) at high temperatures, to
predict the CAT of commercially stored quantities may produce inaccurate results in the range of
40oC above the actual CAT.
Acids:
Weak or dilute acids will react with calcium hypochlorite products to produce chlorate. An exam-
ple of possible pathway for such a reaction has been provided in Eq.3.8, in which hypochlorous
acid reacts with the hypochlorite ion to produce H+-ions, Cl−-ions and chlorate ions. Com-
pounds containing chlorate have strong oxidizing properties, allowing rapid oxidization of
reducing agents. This means that ﬁre involving chlorate may develop rapidly without the supply
of atmospheric oxygen.
2HOCl +OCl−−→2H+ +2Cl−+ClO−
3
(3.8)
Strong acids in excess will cause calcium hypochlorite products to release free chlorine gas, as
described by Eq.3.9. Chlorine gas is toxic to humans as it converts into hydrochloric acid by
reacting with water in the lung tissue, when inhaled.
Ca(OCl)2 +4HX −→CaX2 +2H2O+2Cl2
(3.9)
Reducing agents:
Reducing agents are substances that can be oxidised. These may be organic like glycols or
hydrocarbons, or inorganic like sulphur. From [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981] several reducing agents
where tested for reactivity with hydrated calcium hypochlorite products. A series of potential
==PAGE:50==
50
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
reaction trajectories can be seen in Eq. 3.10, 3.11 & 3.12. These include the chemical products
containing glycols, parafﬁns(hydrocarbons) and sulphur.
Glycol groups can be found chemically as ethylene or propylene glycol. Both chemical are
commercially applied in anti-freeze products, used in the automotive industry as a radiator ﬂuid.
Ethylene glycol is also used as an nonﬂammable hydraulic ﬂuid. In the plastic industry ethylene
glycol is used for producing polyester ﬁbers, while propylene glycol is also applied for producing
polymers it is also utilized as an sweetener in the food industry and a solvent in a range of
pharmaceutical products. On contact with calcium hypochlorite products the glycol group is
oxidized as per Eq. 3.10. This reaction may occur without heating and produce ﬂames and an
explosion, as can be seen in table 3.7 anti-freeze.
HO(CH2)nOH −→HO(CH2)n−1CHO −→HO(CH2)n−1COOH
(3.10)
Parafﬁns(hydrocarbons) covers a range products containing alkanes, which may be categorized
as engine oil, gasoline or solvents. Common for them all is that they contain straight-chain and/or
branched-chain alkanes that follow the general chemical formula CnH2n+2. Alkanes containing
between 5-15 carbon atoms are usually liquid at normal ambient temperature. Product like
gasoline or engine oil will not react spontaneously with calcium hypochlorite products at normal
ambient temperature. However, experiments have shown that parafﬁn oil products will react
with calcium hypochlorite at 50oC ±2oC. The reaction commences with explosive violence and
increase to temperatures of around 220oC [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981].
CnH2n+2 −→nCO2 +n+2H2O
(3.11)
The element sulphur, denoted by the symbol S in the periodic table and occur naturally as sulphur
rings in which 8 sulphur atoms are bounded to each other, i.e. S8. Other variation of sulphur with
fewer or more atoms bounded to each other exist, but the 8 atom variant with its distinct yellow
color is the one found in most abundant quantities. Sulphur can be oxidized and in turn produces
sulphur dioxide SO2, as described by Eq. 3.12. Products containing sulphur range from matches
to powered laundry detergents, but also in heavy fuel oil(HFO) and marine diesel oil(MDO)
that is used for powering the ship’s main engine. HFO may by current standard contain up to
3.5% sulphur by mass. Sulphur can react with the oxygen provided by calcium hypochlorite
products to spontaneously ignite at normal ambient temperature, to produce ﬂames. This has
been shown through experiment conducted in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981]. Whether products like
HFO or MDO, which essentially is a mix of hydrocarbons and sulphur will produce a similar
result has not been evaluated by the cited study.
S −→SO2
(3.12)
A table summarizing on the experiments conducted in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981] have been
created to give an overview of relevant substances that are incompatible with hydrated calcium
hypochlorite products. These are relevant considering the availability aboard container ships,
incompatible in the sense that if calcium hypochlorite products are contaminated with the below
tabulated products it may cause a ﬁre or an explosion. The products contained in the table 3.7
are by no means exhaustive on the subject and is merely an expert of substances experimented
with in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981]. An extract of more incompatible substances can be seen in
appendix A.2 containing the original results from the experiments performed.

3.2.3
Barbecue and hookah charcoal
Several cargo container ﬁres have originated from containers ﬁlled with charcoal for different
purposes. Both charcoal for barbecuing(BBQ) and for heating smoking tobacco have been been
observed to cause ﬁres aboard container ships. Charcoal used for smoking tobacco is often sold
under commercial names like "tablets for water pipes", "shisha charcoal" and "hookah charcoal".
From known cases involving charcoal as the cause of a ﬁres aboard container ships, the most
frequent product initiate these is BBQ charcoal.
Knowledge base
Charcoal itself is a black and light-weight residue that remains after water and volatiles have
been removed from organic matter like wood or coconut shells. These are removed through
pyrolysis or more speciﬁcally carbonisation of biomass. The process is conducted by heating
biomass in an oxygen depleted atmosphere. After the heating process the charcoal mainly consist
of pure carbon, however some ash and volatile matter remain. The volatile matter left in the
charcoal are tar and turpentine products. The amount of volatile components left in the charcoal
after the carbonisation process depends on the temperature at which the process has taken place.
Considering process temperatures around 300oC, 500−600oC and 1000oC the corresponding
yields of charcoal are 50%, 30% and 25%. The latter at 25% yield has a content of volatile
matter close to zero. Charcoal containing a high fraction of volatile matter is easier to ignite, but
produces smoke and ﬂame[Department, 1983].
From the authors research into charcoal used for smoking applications, it was found that
limited scientiﬁc data is available on this speciﬁc subject. The available research was mostly
concerned with the health effects of using it for smoking tobacco. Alternative means for obtaining
data on these charcoal products were considered, and it was decided to obtain this knowledge
from product data sheets published online by manufacturers of such products. Some general
tendencies were discovered from these data sheets and product descriptions, which should be
==PAGE:52==
52
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
highlighted:
• Most of these charcoal products for this niche market are sold as small cylindrical briquettes
made from compacted ﬁnely ground charcoal. They are typically stacked on top of each
other and wrapped in plastic foil. An example of such can be seen in Fig.3.5.
• The types of organic matter carbonized to produce the briquettes range from coconut shells
to hardwood. This seems to vary according to which type of organic matter is available in
the relevant country. Indonesian products tend to rely on coconut husks, while Chinese
sellers utilize wood ﬁbers1.
• Most of the available products have ’quick lighting’ properties. One distributor in USA
describes that this label covers certain additives used for treating the briquette’s surface.
These additives are often potassium or sodium nitrate that serves as an oxidizer and small
quantities of sulphur, which serve as a reducing agent(fuel)2.
Figure 3.5: Picture showing a typical charcoal briquette used for smoking tobacco and its
packaging. Source: www.cn.all.biz/
BBQ charcoal is sold in a number of formats and like the hookah charcoal, the organic
matter used for making it varies between manufacturers. The format of charcoal either resembles
splintered wooden pieces or the typical pillow shaped briquettes. The ﬁrst described is simply
charred wood in varying sizes, also reffered to as ’lump charcoal’ while the briquette products
cover as series charcoal mixes, containing different additives too serve needs of speciﬁc client
segments. One manufacturer called Kingsford located in the USA has listed that their BBQ
briquettes contain the following materials [O’CONNELL, 2000]:
• Wood char - solid fuel, made from charred wood
• Mineral char - solid fuel, made from natural occurring charred biomass
• Mineral carbon - solid fuel, made from natural occurring carbon with limited impurities
like high grade anthracite
• Limestone - makes the ash color white, indicating that the briquette is ready for use
1https://www.alibaba.com/product-detail/apple-wood-shisha-charcoal-no-smoking_60329889385.html
2https://www.b2hookah.com/hookah-coals-process-behind/
==PAGE:53==
3.2 Hazard Identiﬁcation
53
• Starch - binding agent for keeping the fuel and additives together in the briquette shape
• Borax - a mold release agent
• Sodium nitrate - oxidizer enabling easier ignition
• Sawdust - acts as kindling to make ignition easier
A study performed on the self ignition properties of charcoal briquettes [F.C. Wolters, 2003]
concluded that it may occur, but not at normal ambient temperatures for a single bag containing
9 kg of briquettes. For such a bag, an ambient temperature of at least 121oC is required for self
ignition to occur. From their investigation the critical ambient temperature was determined as
function of the bag’s radius. As an example parameters of a standard 9 kg bag of charcoal with
a radius of 13 cm is visible in the graph provided by their study and presented in Fig.3.6. In
addition to this the authors have marked the typical minimum radius inside a general purpose
shipping contain and the corresponding minimum critical ambient temperature into the graph,
respectively 1.25m and 325K or 325oC.
Figure 3.6: Stability map for bagged wood char. Source: [F.C. Wolters, 2003]
The above available knowledge on charcoal and wood char is in no way exhaustive, but
will serve as the basis of understanding what hazards are relevant when transporting charcoal.
The shear variety of charcoal products available makes the task of mapping these non-feasible
and in any case beyond the scope of this thesis. The hazards concerning charcoal products will
therefore be dealt with in more general terms.
Hazards
Even though the hazards are dealt with through a more general approach that doesn’t mean
charcoal doesn’t pose some speciﬁc hazard. These relate to self-heating, bacterial induced
heating, the oxidizers some are treated with and toxic combustion products of charcoal.
Self-ignition
As described self-ignition is a potential hazard when transporting charcoal at elevated temper-
atures in greater quantities than simply a bag. As given account for in previous section, heat
sources are widely available in the cargo hold and above the weather deck that are able to produce
ambient temperatures of 50oC and above.
Bacterial induced heating
Fires in Hay and wood chip piles are not uncommon throughout the summer season. The cause
of these are mostly attributed to bacterial activity, producing signiﬁcant amounts heat that cause
==PAGE:54==
54
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
auto-ignition of bacterial metabolic products and the bulk material. Similar effects have been
observed in charcoal. From [Department, 1983] bacterial activity is also possible in charcoal.
It is however estimated that bacterial activity in charcoal terminates at 80oC, causing limited
issues for at 9 kg bag of charcoal, but does exceed the critical temperature estimated for a general
purpose shipping container carrying charcoal.
Effects of oxidizers
The fact that some charcoal products are treated with oxidizing agents to ease ignition at the end
user, could have an effect on the initial stages of a charcoal ﬁre. Even if the conﬁned space of the
general purpose cargo container is entirely air-tight, the initial combustion would have a source
of oxygen because of the oxidizing agents.
Toxicity
Incomplete combustion of charcoal produces signiﬁcant amounts of carbon monoxide(CO).
There are several well documented cases of people having indoor barbecues and dying from
CO asphyxiation. Staying in areas with concentrations of 800ppm may cause death within 2-3
hours, while 6400ppm causes dizziness, nausea and thinking impairment within minutes and
death within 10-15 minutes.
3.2.4
Rechargeable batteries
From the research on cargo container ﬁres, only two cases were identiﬁed to originate from
rechargeable batteries. One was caused by lithium-ion batteries while the other was caused by
nickel metal-hydride batteries. Although, rechargeable batteries aren’t the most frequent cause
of cargo ﬁres aboard container ships, the fact that batteries will become evermore present in
consumer products like electric vehicles and home power storage in combination with solar cells,
makes the cases quite relevant.
Knowledge base
Rechargeable batteries have been available since 1890’s. These batteries were lead-acid batteries.
Such batteries are still seen in cars as it is possible to draw large currents from them for short
periods of time. Current rechargeable batteries for consumer electronics and electric cars are
based mainly on nickel-metal hydride(NiMH) and Lithium-ion technology(LiB). A great deal of
research and money is being invested into the development of new battery types, to enhance their
capacity, reduce the size and enhance lifespan. As research into the subject of cargo container
ﬁres aboard container ships have only revealed NiMH and LiB to be the cause of ﬁres involving
batteries. Therefore the hazard identifying scope concerning batteries has been limited to these
two types.
The main purpose of a battery is to store an electrical charge. This is achieved in the
electrochemical cells that store energy through a reversible electrochemical reactions. A single
electrochemical cell is comprised of a cathode, an anode and a electrolytic compound. LiB
and NiMH are different in respect to the materials and functionality of these. The LiB consist
of a cathode and anode manufactured from metal oxide and porous carbon(graphite or coke)
respectively. When the battery is discharged the lithium-ions travel from the anode to the cathode,
meaning that the anode is subjected to oxidation(loss of electrons) while the cathode undergoes
reduction(a gain of electrons. This process is also described by Fig.3.7.
==PAGE:55==
3.2 Hazard Identiﬁcation
55
Figure 3.7: Schematics describing the working principle of lithium-ion batteries, while charging
and discharging. Source: Batteryuniversity.com
The transport of lithium-ions is conducted by an electrolytic compound between the cath-
ode and anode. This is in most cases a mix of organic solvents, lithium salts and a range of
proprietary additives to enhance battery life-span and performance. For most LiBs the organic
solvents ethyl methyl carbonate(EMC) and dimethyl carbonate(DMC) are utilized in dissolving
the lithium salts [Vegge, 2015]. Organic solvents like EMC and DMC are highly ﬂammable,
toxic, volatile and have ﬂash points in the range of 16oC to 33oC. A study on ﬂammability
of LiB’s electrolytes was conducted in [Steffen Hess and Wachtler, 2014], producing results on
individual solvents and electrolytic compounds ﬂash point, vapor pressure and self-extinguishing
time. Tabulated data from the experiments conducted in [Steffen Hess and Wachtler, 2014]
have been included in App.A.3. Electrolytic salts used in LiBs also vary between manufactur-
ers, but lithium hexaﬂuorophosphate(LiPF6), lithium bistriﬂuoromethanesulfonimidate(LiTFSI,
CF3SO2NLiSO2CF3) and lithium bis(oxalate)borate(LiBOB, LiB(C2O4)2) are widely applied
for commercial production of lithium-ion batteries.
NiMH batteries is one of the most common rechargeable consumer batteries, and is widely
available in AA and AAA formats as an alternative to single use alkaline batteries. The electro-
chemical charging and discharging reaction has been presented in Eq.3.13, following the arrow
right shows the discharging reaction an vice verse for charging.
MH +NiOOH ⇆M +Ni(OH)2
(3.13)
M is an metallic alloy, often mixture of rare earth metals like cerium(Ce), lanthanum(La),
praseodymium(Pr), neodymium(Nd). The electrolyte in NiMH batteries is usually potassium
hydroxide, which in contrast to LiB’s electrolyte is not ﬂammable. Upon charging, discharging
or subjecting the battery to temperatures higher than normal ambient temperature, may cause
the internal pressure of the battery to increase relative to its surroundings. To prevent NiMH
batteries from bulging or rupturing, they are ﬁtted with a safety valve located at the positive
contact. This can be seen in Fig.3.8, where it is marked as ’vent mechanism’.
==PAGE:56==
56
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.8: Diagram showing a typical inner structure NiMH. Source: Energizer.com
Hazards
From the research conducted on the battery types lithium-ion batteries and NiMH a series of
hazards were discovered, relating to transportation and storage of these. Further, hazards arise if
the batteries are subjected to a ﬁre will be presented as well.
Lithium-ion batteries(LiB)
The hazard surrounding LiBs relate to rapid thermal runaway reactions that commence internally
in the battery. Such an event may be caused by a several effects, such as short-circuiting the
positive and negative terminals of the battery, external heating or puncturing the battery. There
are several cases of such reactions occurring. The best known might be the Samsung Galaxy
Note 7 smartphones. In 2016 the Korean smartphone producer Samsung decided to recall
approximately 2.5 million of this speciﬁc model, due to several cases of its battery catching
ﬁre. It was later discovered and announced by Samsung that this speciﬁc fault was caused by
design error, which on occasion caused the terminals of the battery to short-circuit. In the next
paragraphs the hazards relating to heating, short-circuiting and puncturing will be explained.
Heat:
A study from 2013 [Andrey W. Golubkov and et al., 2013] dealt with estimating the onset tem-
perature that would cause a 18650 lithium battery cell to produce thermal runaway. The tests
were conducted on batteries containing 3 different cathode materials, which were LFP, NMC and
NMC/LCO. Test were conducted in a temperature controlled pressure vessel containing an inert
gas atmosphere(argon). From the study it was concluded that the temperature that caused thermal
runaway was approximately 195oC, 170oC and 150oC respectively for the 3 different cathode
materials. The thermal runaway reaction caused a temperature increase of approx. 210oC, 500oC
and 700oC relative to the onset temperature causing the thermal runaway reaction. Further, all of
the investigated batteries produced combustible and/or toxic gasses during the tests. These were
carbon dioxide, hydrogen, carbon monoxide, methane, ethane and ethylene in varying amounts.
==PAGE:57==
3.2 Hazard Identiﬁcation
57
Further, the study speculated that hydrogen ﬂuoride was produced, but the facilities were not
equipped to measure such chemicals. Hydrogen ﬂuoride is highly corrosive and reactive, upon
contact with moisture. The gas may cause rapid blindness upon contact with corneas of an eye.
Short-circuit of terminals:
Short-circuiting or high discharge rates of any battery is ill advised, as the electrochemical
process inside the battery produces heat; this phenomenon is called resistive heating. Parts of the
battery having high impedance, like weld spots and electrode surfaces are most susceptible to
heating. The separating liner between the cathode and anode is usually made from polyethylene
or similar. Therefore, the upper boundary of the battery’s resistance towards temperature is
linked to its durability at elevated temperatures. Collapse of the liner has potential to produce an
event where the battery vents with ﬂame, as the ﬂammable electrolytes may be ignited by the
anode and cathodic materials producing heat and/or sparks.
Puncturing:
Puncturing a LIB, much in the same way as collapse of the liner, produces a situation in which
the anode and cathode materials are in direct contact. This essentially produces a short-circuit
and subsequently a lot of heat and perhaps even sparks. This would make the LIB vent through
the location of the puncture, either with or without ﬂame.
Nickel metal hydride(NiMH)
Hazards relating to NiMH-batteries are signiﬁcantly different to that of LIBs, as the electrolyte
isn’t ﬂammable and they produce small quantities of hydrogen when discharged. Relevant
hazards of NiMH-batteries will be presented in the below paragraphs.
Heat:
Storing NiMH-batteries at high ambient temperatures may cause the cells to electrically dis-
charge. This process may produce hydrogen gas, due to limited capacity of the nickel electrode.
Subsequently high temperatures may increase the internal pressure of the battery, in which
case the safety valve would alleviate this. The expelled gasses would contain small amounts of
hydrogen, which has the lower and upper ﬂammable limits of 4% and 75 % respectively. Further,
the ignition energy required is very low for hydrogen, mean that the probability of ignition would
be quite high.
Short-circuit:
In the event of a short-circuit, signiﬁcant heat may be evolved internally in battery and the
short-circuiting material would in many cases if metal become glowing hot. The rapid discharge
of the battery, whould also evolve hydrogen gas. This therefor provides the same hazards as
above.
Pyrophoric materials:
The metal hydrides used in the negative electrode of NiMH-batteries have pyrophoric tendencies,
which means that if the airtight outer shell of the battery is damaged it may spontaneously ignite
at ambient temperatures lower than 55oC.
3.2.5
Divinylbenzene
Divinylbenzene has been identiﬁed as the potential cause of one major cargo container ﬁre during
the investigations of this thesis. Its chemical properties makes it polymerize under the right
circumstances. As this characteristic applies to other compounds being shipped as well and may
produces hazardous thermal runaway reactions it was decided to identify hazards associated with
divinylbenzene.
==PAGE:58==
58
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Knowledge base
Divinylbenzene (DVB) has the chemical formula C10H10, and is a yellowish liquid at normal
ambient temperatures. It consists of a benzene ring on which two vinyl groups are attached. Its
practical uses includes increasing the strength of adhesives, lowering shrinkage of thermosetting
resin in composite plastics and increasing skid-resistance in thermoplastic rubber. The ﬂash point
of DVB is 70oC and it has an auto-ignition temperature of 470oC. The vapours of DVB have a
Lower ﬂammability limit of 1.1% and an upper Upper Flammablity Limit of 6.2%. To extinguish
a ﬁre originating from DVB carbon dioxide, foam or dry chemical are the most effective. Water
is in general considered ineffective when dealing with such a ﬁre; this also applies to water
fog and ﬁne spray water extinguishing devices. When utilizing foam extinguishing system, the
preferred foam types are general purpose synthetic foams or protein foams. General provisions
on working with, storing, extinguishing and transporting DVB was obtained from product data
sheets and commercial presentations of the product [DOW, 2000].
Hazards
Like any other ﬂammable hydrocarbon product DVB poses a range of hazards, but unlike gasoline
or petrol based clean products it may also react with it self and polymerize which develops heat.
Carrier leakage:
Vapors of DVB are heavier than air and very ﬂammable, but as the product doesn’t have a high
vapor pressure at normal ambient temperatures, its transgression into vapors is limited. Elevated
temperatures changes this and signiﬁcant amount of vapor may be produced. Such vapors have a
higher density than air and may travel considerable distances to ignition sources and cause ﬂash
ﬁres or explosions.
Heat:
To avoid hazardous polymerization, bulk DVB is provided with an inhibitor called tertiary-butyl-
catechol (TBC) in concentrations at of at least 1000 ppm . Further at least 10 ppm of oxygen
needs to be dissolved in the DVB for the TBC to be effective. If the product is stored for longer
than 6 weeks at 27oC the levels of inhibitor and oxygen must be maintained. This includes
aerating it once a week with dry air. Depletion of TBC occurs naturally even at low temperatures,
but accelerates the higher they get. The relation between TBC depletion is visible in table 3.8.
Table 3.8: Storage recommendations for DVB, concerning oxygen and TCB inhibitor levels
Storage
Temp. [oC]
Storage

1
If the levels of inhibitor or oxygen are not maintained the monomers will begin to polymerize
producing heat. Initially the heat may be transferred out of the carrier vessel via convection,
but as the polymerization commences DVB becomes more viscous, making the heat transfer
less efﬁcient. If heat isn’t lost the exothermic reaction may auto-accelerate, producing a rapid
temperature rise, increased internal pressure, vigorous venting of container and ﬁre or explosion if
not arrested. Quantitatively the polymerization produces 88–92 kJ of heat per mole of monomer.
==PAGE:59==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
59
Contamination:
DVB may react violently if it’s contaminated with a series of materials. The products that it is
incompatible with are:
• Acids, e.g. hydrochloric or sulphuric acid
• Oxidizing materials, e.g. calcium hypochlorite or organic peroxides
• Metallic halide salts, e.g. Sodium Chloride
• Polymerization catalysts
• Clay absorbents, e.g. like clay cat litter
• Copper and copper alloys, e.g. wiring or brass components
Toxicity:
Combustion of DVB produces a series of products that are toxic in various degrees. As with
the combustion any product containing hydrocarbons, carbon dioxide(CO2) is produced. Also
carbon monoxide will be produced as a completely stoichiometric combustion, producing CO2
and water, is unlikely in the event of a pool ﬁre. Further, incomplete combustion may also
produce toxic fumes and mists of the original product, which may cause suffocation or chemical
pneumonia.
3.3
Cause consequence analysis
In order to analyze the risk associated with the containerized shipment of the identiﬁed hazards, a
cause consequence study was conducted in collaboration with risk analyst J. Robert Taylor. The
study allows the authors to investigate the occurrence of critical events by analyzing potential
causes for the realization of such events combined with the consequence chains arising from
these hazardous events. A representation of the overall structure of the analysis is given in ﬁgure
3.9.
Figure 3.9: Representation of the overall structure of the cause consequence study
3.3.1
Methodology
Cause consequence analysis (CCA) is an extension of fault tree analysis (FTA) and event tree
analysis (ETA) . This enables a combined investigation into cause analysis through the FTA and
consequence analysis via ETA. Herby deductive and inductive analysis can be performed.
The purpose of such CCA is to determine the cause consequence relations that may arise from an
identiﬁed critical event. An example of the general CCA procedure can be seen in ﬁgure 3.10.
==PAGE:60==
60
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.10: An example of a CCA template/procedure, showing the typical approach when
documenting CCA
The presented cause consequence template/diagram is built up by so-called decision boxes.
Each decision box deﬁnes a system condition or event, which can either be a cause for the
occurrence of the critical event, the critical event itself or the consequence chains arising from
the critical event. Some of the decision boxes may contain ‘YES-NO’ branches, which are
commonly used for the structuring of ETA. Following the individual ‘YES-NO’ branching
different consequence chains are developed. Each decision branch triggers an event between the
critical event and the ﬁnal consequence damages.
3.3.2
Cause consequence study
Four cause consequence studies were conducted in order to analyze the risk associated with
the containerized shipment of the cargoes which were identiﬁed as the main hazards to the
ﬁre safety aboard container ships; calcium hypochlorite, charcoal, rechargeable batteries and
divinylbenzene. Each of the produced cause consequence diagrams will be presented in the
following sections along with elaborations on the discovered cause-consequence relations.
Further the cause-consequence studies will be applied to investigate patterns among the cause-
consequence relations and deduce on general frequencies of escalation path by comparing the
obtained relations with the investigated real-life ﬁre scenarios.
Cause consequence diagram - Calcium Hypochlorite
The sequential structure of the cause consequence diagram allows a visual and logical representa-
tion of the cause-consequence relations achieved through the conducted risk analyzes. The entire
cause consequence diagram has been placed in appendix A.4.1 due to its size, it presents the
cause consequence study conducted for the decomposition of calcium hypochlorite. Individual
part hereof has been presented in the following paragraphs.
Critical event & cause of critical event
The critical or primary event investigated in this study was the decomposition of calcium
hypochlorite. As described in section 3.2.2 anhydrous and hydrated calcium hypochlorite
product, respectively UN2880 and UN1748, are relatively stable products at room normal
ambient temperature. Contaminants, higher than normal ambient temperature, packaging sizes,
==PAGE:61==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
61
moisture does however inﬂuence the stability of calcium hypochlorite products in a potentially
hazardous way. From the cause part of the CCD ﬁgure 3.12 several trajectories to the critical
event exist. These are based on the knowledge displayed in section 3.2.2.
Figure 3.11: Causes of critical event - Decomposition of calcium hypochlorite
When transporting calcium hypochlorite by sea in a general purpose cargo container a
number of causes can be identiﬁed for the decomposition of calcium hypochlorite. Whether
the container is positioned above or below the weather deck of the container ship, there is a
probability that it may be subjected to external heat sources. Assessing the consequence part of
the CCD, a series of possible external and internal heat sources have been identiﬁed. Addressing
• External heat sources: are present throughout the cargo storage areas of a container ship.
This is also acknowledged by the IMDG-Code, which has implemented provision where
calcium hypochlorite products should be stored.
– Above the weather deck: Infra-red(IR) solar radiation has been identiﬁed as a
major contributor in heating up general purpose cargo containers. As described in
section 3.2.2, temperatures inside a general purpose container can reach 50oC from
effects like this alone. In terms of machinery, reefer containers may also serve as a
heat source as the integral refrigeration unit’s condenser is designed to remove heat
from the reefer containers internal space.
– Below the weather deck: more heat-sources are available, the sun does however
have limited effect on these containers. As seen in [section on cont. ship structure
REF] the areas below the weather deck houses a series machinery, piping and equip-
ment in general, which are critical for normal operation of a container ship. Most
container ships operating internationally use HFO as the primary fuel for propulsion3.
The HFO storage and settling tanks are equipped with heating coils that usually
carry steam to heat the surrounding fuel oil. Storage tank are, by recommendation
of class societies, heated to approximately 10oC above the fuel oil’s pour point,
which for most standard fuel oils is approximately 30oC. Further, settling tanks are
heated to 60oC and more and in many cases the surface temperature is in the range of
70-80oC, as estimated by the German Federal Bureau of Maritime Causualty Inves-
tigation(BSU) in their investigations of the ﬁre aboard CMV PUNJAB SENATOR
[Kaufmann, 2006].
3Some ships use e.g. Liquid Natural Gas(LNG) or lower viscosity fuel oils as their primary fuel. These do not
require heating while being stored
==PAGE:62==
62
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
– Below the weather deck: machinery found in the engine room and reefers, as
explained above contribute to the overall heat-ﬂux into the cargo spaces.
– Slow decomposition and high insulation: considers a scenario in which the cal-
cium hypochlorite product is either tightly packed or placed in reefer container
without an active refrigerating unit or a defective unit. Tightly packed cargo enables
limited convection between the individual items, effectively insulating goods furthest
away from the walls of the container. This gives rise to the effect described in
[Halliburton, 2002], which stipulates that increasing the bulk volume of stored cal-
cium hypochlorite reduces the critical ambient temperature(CAT) required to produce
a thermal runaway reaction. The secondary trajectory, involving an insulated reefer
container with a defective or unplugged refrigeration unit, provides an environment
which provides limited heat-ﬂux via radiation or heat-transfer via natural convection
to the surroundings. An instances like this would essentially trap the heat generated
by packed calcium hypochlorite, in turn increasing the rate of its heat generation, i.e.
reaction rate, potentially causing a thermal runaway reaction.
• External ﬁres: in near proximity to general purpose containers carrying calcium hypochlo-
rite products, could produce heat ﬂux-my not be right CHECK and transfer heat via natural
convention in quantities great enough initiate decomposition of said product. As calcium
hypochlorite products are prone to decomposition at elevated temperatures, this also poses
a trajectory for the critical event, i.e. decomposition of HClO.
External or internal non-directly temperature related effects like contaminants or moisture
may also provide a pathway to the critical event, i.e. decomposition of Ca(ClO)2.
• Moisture and Ultra violet radiation: may affect calcium hypochlorite products differ-
ently depending on the initial moisture content. Anhydrous calcium hypochlorite(UN
1748) has from studies in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981] been shown to produce more heat
than a equivalent hydrated calcium hypochlorite(UN2880) product, upon being subjected
the same quantities of water. Information on the governing reactions an hazards of this
subject was explained in section 3.2.2. Further, the presence of moisture(water) in the
above mentioned calcium hypochlorite products produce hypochlorous acid(Eq.3.3, which
in turn cause an increase in levels of chlorate(strong oxidizer). Ultra violet breaks the
HClO molecule into chloride ions, among other causing an increase in chlorate quantities,
producing the same issue as above. This is however unlikely to happen while the product
is carried in a shipping container. It is more likely that it may have been subjected to UV
radiation prior to being loaded.
• Contaminants: introduced into calcium hypochlorite products have from experiments
conducted in [Arthur C. Kennett, 1981] proved to produce spontaneous combustion of the
introduced contaminant. Especially reducing agents may cause such an event to occur, as
explained in section 3.2.2. Pathways for contaminant to enter e.g. a general purpose cargo
container have not been considered in details, but from an practical and logical point of
view it has been considered unlikely that these are introduced after the container has been
sealed.
Consequence of critical event
==PAGE:63==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
63
Figure 3.12: Consequence of critical event - Decomposition of calcium hypochlorite
==PAGE:64==
64
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
The decomposition of calcium hypchlorite has been recognized as an critical event and the
realization of such an event can trigger a number of consequence chains. These chains of conse-
quence are presented in ﬁgure 3.12. The ﬁrst system condition or followup-event considered
after calcium hypochlorite has started to decompose within a container is a "safeguard". The
decision box "safe guard" deﬁnes a system condition which could terminate the decomposition
process. Such a safeguard or safety barrier could be created by shipping the dangerous cargo in a
reefer container which would keep the climatic temperature within the container at a minimum.
This would remove the heat generated during the exothermic decomposition process and thereby
cause the decomposition process of the cargo in question to terminate. Alternatively, considering
that the case concerns a general purpose cargo container, natural convection may be adequate in
disposing of the excess heat. In either case, as the system is not able generate enough internal
energy to distort the heat-balance, the system will not progress to a state of thermal runaway,
thereby ending the consequence chain.
If this condition is not satisﬁed, the self-heating process caused by the chemical reaction will
progress which would result in a thermal runaway. If thermal runaway occurs, the probability of
a ﬁre igniting within the container is high, as the reaction could cause the cargo to self ignite. If
this condition is satisﬁed and the ﬁre develops, the next event in the consequence chain would
be the detection of the ﬁre. If the ﬁre is not detected the ﬁre will gradually escalate over time.
Depending on the severity and size of the internal ﬁre, the heat transfer between the closed
stacked container could cause the cargo of the adjacent containers to ignite. Regardless of the
ﬁre affecting adjacent containers or not, the ﬁre will continue to escalate until the ﬁre eventually
is detected. At the point of detection an initial emergency response would be initiated, in order
to access the situation and determine the extent of the ﬁre. If this assessment is not possible,
due to access difﬁculties, the delay in detection etc. the ﬁre would continue to escalate until the
assessment could be carried out.
Depending on the amount of smoke emitted from the container at the point of carrying out
the initial emergency response (presumably toxic, due to the chemical reaction), the emergency
crew could be subjected to a hazardous environment. If no measures are taken to protect the
emergency response crew from this toxic environment, the effect of the toxic gasses emitted
from the chemical ﬁre could be fatal.
Considering the emergency response executed successfully without being affected by the toxic
smoke and a proper assessment of the ﬁre had been carried out, a ﬁre ﬁghting response would
subsequently be initiated. The rules and regulations deﬁned by SOLAS requires the ship to
carry at least two SCBA sets. If the decision box "SCBA effective" is not satisﬁed, which
could be caused by leaking SCBA, issues with the compressor applied for ﬁlling the SCBA
or not enough SCBA available to secure the whole ﬁre ﬁghting response team, the endanger-
ment to the ﬁre ﬁghting response team would again be fatal as a consequence of the toxic
smoke. Considering the SCBA effective, the ﬁreﬁghters equipped with these will be secure
from the toxic environment, thereby allowing them to initiate the actual ﬁre suppression response.
If the ﬁre ﬁghting response is effective and the crew are able to sufﬁciently suppress the ﬁre,
limited damage will come to the ship, crew and the containerized cargo. On the other hand if the
crew are not able to extinguish the ﬁre with the suppression arrangements available aboard the
container ship, the ﬁre will continue to escalate and spread around the ship. This would cause
extensive damage to the ship and the containerized cargo and potentially be fatal to the crew
members if they are not properly evacuated.
==PAGE:65==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
65

the discovered cases, only contain limited information about the cause relations and escalation

scenarios. Each of the ﬁre scenarios applied was assigned a number, as illustrated below, which
was applied to illustrate and deﬁne causes and the path of escalation of the individual real-life
ﬁre scenarios on the attained cause consequence diagram.

==PAGE:67==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
67
By initially focusing on the causes which lead to the occurrence of the actual ﬁre incidents
aboard the investigated container ships, one can see that limited information is available about
this phase. The reason might be that following the ignition of such ﬁres, a rapid or slow ﬁre
progression will occur, which is also mirrored through the cause consequence relations. The
progression of these ﬁres will often make it difﬁcult to determine the actual cause of ignition as
most of the evidence is lost as a consequence of the ﬁre. This also emphasizes the importance of
conducting these risk analysis, as these improve the ability to predict the general causes for the
ignition of ﬁres, thereby allowing safety engineers to reduce the probabilities of these ﬁres by
trying to manage or mitigate the discovered causes.
Through investigation of the 10 discovered real-life ﬁre scenarios it was possible to identify
the actual cause of the critical event for three of the real-life ﬁre scenarios, namely case 2, 6
and 4. In case 2 and 6 the cause of the ﬁre/explosion was discovered to be due to a container
carrying calcium hypochlorite that had been stored next to a HFO tank, causing the internal
temperature of the container to increase during the voyage of the container ship. The ﬁre incident
in case 4 was allegedly caused as a result of impurities in the stored calcium hypochlorite which
caused the cargo to self-combust leading to an explosion of the container followed by a severe ﬁre.
For all three of the ﬁre scenarios it was possible to mirror the actual cause of the real life
ﬁre event (critical event), with one of the causes identiﬁed for the attained cause consequence
diagrams.
When comparing the consequence chains attained in the cause consequence diagram with
the actual escalation paths of the real life ﬁre scenarios, one can see that almost all of the ﬁre
incidents can be linked to the same path of escalation. Further, it can be seen that all of the
discovered ﬁre incidents, which initiated as a consequence of the decomposition of calcium
hypochlorite, resulted in major damages to the ship and containerized cargo, except for one. This
supports the authors identiﬁcation of calcium hypochlorite, as being a high consequence hazard
to the ﬁre safety aboard container ships, when shipped in containers.
==PAGE:68==
68
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Cause consequence diagram - Charcoal
The cause consequence diagram attained through the cause consequence study conducted for the
containerized transport of charcoal is presented in appendix A.4.2. Parts of the cause consequence
diagram are presented in the following paragraphs.
Critical event & cause of critical event
Figure 3.14: Diagram showing the causes of thermal runaway in packed charcoal
As previously established charcoal is prone to self-ignite if kept in large densely packed
quantities at elevated ambient temperatures. This may produce a situation in which thermal
runaway occurs. As thermal runaway is a condition in which more heat is generated inside the
system then is lost to the surroundings, causing an elevated temperature inside the system and
may lead to self-ignition of the charcoal. Thermal runaway has therefore been identiﬁed as the
sole critical event through hazard identiﬁcation. This state may be caused by several different
initiators. Most are not different from the causes described in previous cause consequence
analysis and will therefore only be summarized. To present the causes in an clear fashion the
cause part of the cause consequent analysis diagram is present in ﬁgure 3.14.
Established in the hazard identiﬁcation was that densely packed bags of charcoal with a ra-
dius of 1.25 m may produce conditions, where the CAT is reduced to approximately 52oC. Some
scenarios may produce ambient temperatures in this vicinity. Below deck this includes heating
from adjacent HFO-tanks, steam, external ﬁres and various machinery. Above the weather
deck the the main external heat source is the sun. Hot stow cargo may be heated sufﬁciently to
experience internal temperatures in the area of the CAT. Signiﬁcantly different to these are a
couple of causes relating to internal self-heating, these are applicable both above and below the
weather deck.
• Bacterial heating:charcoal may be exposed to bacterial activity, as described in [F.C. Wolters, 2003].
==PAGE:69==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
69
Such activity may increase the internal temperature of the charcoal, up to 80oC. As this is
well above the CAT established previously, this may well be a cause of thermal runaway.
• Vibration induced friction: Vibrations are present throughout a ship, in varying degrees.
These are produced by the main engine, auxiliary machinery, pressure induced on the hull
surface by the propeller and waves acting on the hull. Vibrations could cause internal
friction between the individual charcoal briquettes. This could produce areas in-between
the briquettes with high temperatures and possibly thermal ignition. Such a scenario could
therefore very well cause the critical event, i.e. thermal runaway.
Consequence of critical event
Considering the critical event of a thermal runaway reaction process caused by the containerized
storage of charcoal, a number of consequence chains may arise. These chains of consequence
are presented in ﬁgure 3.15. The ﬁrst follow-up event considered as a consequence of the
thermal runaway reaction, is the ignition of the charcoal. If the system condition is not satisﬁed
and the self-heating process is terminated the charcoal will cease to ignite, thereby ending the
consequence chain with no further escalation. If the charcoal ignites due to thermal runaway and
the ﬁre is allowed to evolve or grow, the probability of the ﬁre igniting the packing material and
the pallets it is stored upon is to be considered. If the material surrounding the charcoal is not
ignited the escalation process is considered terminated. Opposite if the ﬁre continues to grow
and spreads, two parallel consequence chains arises.
The immediate consequence of the growing ﬁre, is that the ﬁre is left uncontrolled which
thereby constitutes the chance of the ﬁre reaching an energy level where the heat will transfer to
adjacent containers. In parallel with the ﬁre progression process within the sealed container, the
ﬁre will at some point in the ﬁre development process, reach a detectable level. When this system
condition is satisﬁed an initial emergency response will be initiated in order to asses the ﬁre
situation and the ﬁre ﬁghting conditions/possibilities for termination. Until a proper assessment
of the ﬁre is conducted, limited measures can be taken to suppress the ﬁre, thereby allowing
the ﬁre to further progress. At the point where the emergency assessment is deemed properly
executed, strategic planning of the actual ﬁre ﬁghting response can be conducted. If the ﬁre is
considered to have progressed to such a state where a ﬁre ﬁghting response by the crew would
be inadequate to terminate the ﬁre, no ﬁre ﬁghting response would be initiated. Instead the ﬁre
would be left to progress while the crew began an evacuation mission from the burning ship.
==PAGE:70==
70
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.15: Consequence of critical event - thermal runaway charcoal
==PAGE:71==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
71
Considering the ﬁre to be in a state, in development process, where the crew were still able
to contain or extinguish it, a ﬁre ﬁghting response would be initiated. The effectiveness of
the SCBA sets are crucial to the success a ﬁre response mission. Charcoal ﬁres often causes
the dispersion of toxic smoke and gasses. The toxic environment covering the area around the
container would therefore impose a great endangerment to the crew, in a situation were the the
SCBA sets were ineffective.
If the system condition is satisﬁed and the SCBA sets are effective, the effectiveness of the actual
response can be considered. The ﬁre ﬁghting response is considered effective if the crew are
able to contain or extinguish the ﬁre before it is able to spread to adjacent containers. In this case
the damages caused by the ﬁre would be limited to the burning container and therefore limited
damage would be considered inﬂicted.
If the system condition is not satisﬁed and the ﬁre spreads to adjacent containers, the ﬁre
ﬁghting response is considered ineffective. In this case the ﬁre is expected to inﬂict major
damages to the ship, containerized cargo and possibly endanger the lives of the crew members.
Further it could force the crew to abandon the ship as the ﬁre, in worst case, could cause the ship
to sink.
General frequencies of escalation path - known container ship ﬁres
A total of 7 real-life ﬁre scenarios were discovered to have been initiated as a consequence of the
containerized shipment and storage of charcoal. In the attempt to investigate patterns among the
causes and deduce on general frequencies of escalation path of the ﬁres, a similar approach to
the one presented for the shipment of calcium hypochlorite was conducted. An overview of the
investigated real-life ﬁre scenarios with the assigned identiﬁcation numbers are presented below.
[1] HANSA CLIPPER - YEAR 1996
[2] MARLENE S. - YEAR 1996
[3] BELLATRIX - YEAR 1997
[4] CAROLINE MAERSK - YEAR 2015
[5] MARENO - YEAR 2015
[6] MSC KATRINA - YEAR 2015
[7] LUDWIGSHAFEN EXPRESS - YEAR 2016
An overview of the attained cause consequence diagram with cause relations and escalation
paths of the individual real-life ﬁre scenarios included, is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.16.
==PAGE:72==
72
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.16: Cause consequence diagram for thermal runaway of charcoal with included cause
relations and escalation paths for the real-life ﬁre scenarios
==PAGE:73==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
73
Through investigation of the documentation available for the 7 individual ﬁre scenarios, it
was not possible to uncover the actual cause of ignition of the charcoal. Only that the stored
cargo which caused the ﬁre incidents was charcoal. In case 4, DMAIB were able to conclude
that the ignition of the charcoal was caused as a consequence of self-heating of the charcoal,
which was possible to mirror in the attained cause consequence diagram.
Similarities in the paths of escalation of the ﬁres were detected from the attained diagram.
Almost all the ﬁres resulted in the ﬁre spreading to one or several adjacent containers. In most of
the cases the adjacent containers, affected by the ﬁre, was also stored with charcoal.
As previously explained the deﬁnition "major damages" deﬁnes an event where the conse-
quences of the ﬁre leads to extensive damage or destruction of the stored containerized cargo
and possibly the container ship. Further, "major damages" could include the ship being forced to
prematurely dock as a consequence of the ﬁre. This would result in unforeseen down time of the
ship and thereby constitute lost revenue (ﬁnancial loss/damage).
From the diagram it can be seen that all of the discovered escalations paths lead to the ﬁ-
nal event of major damages. This substantiates the importance of correct handling and storage
when transporting containerized charcoal. The shipper should at all cost attempt to avoid expos-
ing the containers stored with charcoal to any of the identiﬁed causes, as these could provoke
thermal runaway and thereby a consequence chain emanating from the ignition of the charcoal.
By limiting or mitigating the causes of the critical event the probability of experiencing the
consequence chains can be reduced, which would help to improve the ﬁre safety aboard the
container ships.
Cause consequence diagram - Lithium-ion batteries
Hazards of storing and transporting rechargeable batteries were presented in section 3.2.4. As a
signiﬁcant difference in hazards between LiBs and NiMH-batteries was discovered the cause
consequence analysis was only performed on LiBs. This section will consider a thermal runaway
reaction in Lithium-ion batteries. Due to size limitations the cause consequence diagram was
place in appendix A.4.3. Parts of the diagram are presented below.
Critical event & cause of critical event in relation to lithium-ion batteries
==PAGE:74==
74
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.17: Diagram showing the causes of thermal runaway in lithium-ion batteries
The critical event identiﬁed as most likely to occur, through the hazard identiﬁcation in
Sec.3.2.4 was a thermal runaway reaction. The causes of such an event varies to some extent but
essentially it occurs when the anode and cathode come in direct physical contact with each other.
First cause considered is a short-circuited battery, which as explained might produce enough
heat to melt the polyethylene liner. Trajectories for producing a short-circuit are:
• Short-circuit of battery cell
– Vibrations: rubbing between batteries might cause insulating materials be worn away,
providing a means for short-circuiting.
– Bad design or assembly: as with the batteries in the Samsung smart phones that
vented with ﬂame, it is not unlikely that a battery assembled incorrectly or produced
with an faulty design might exhibit the same tendencies producing an internal short-
circuit.
– Insufﬁcient isolation or packaging: considering a scenario like the one aboard CMA
CGM Rossini where the electrical isolation between individual battery terminals
failed, enabling several or individual battery’s terminals to touch and cause a short-
circuit. Alternatively, inadequate external packaging or internal segregation of the
batteries might cause them to tumble around inside the container or internally in the
packaging, allowing for the terminals to touch one another.
• Punctured cell
– Mechanical impact: rough handling or rough handling of the batteries in combination
with sharp edges might potentially puncture the battery cell, causing the cathode and
==PAGE:75==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
75
anode of the cell to interfere, while penetrating the insulating plastic liner.
– Inadequate packaging: batteries that are allowed to rub against each other, in combi-
nation with e.g. sharp edges, might penetrate the cells of adjacent batteries. Further,
breached packaging could result in loose batteries inside the shipping container
where they could collide with sharp edges causing penetration of the cell.
• External heat source: a series of potential heat sources have been dealt with in previous
cause consequence analysis and in the hazard identiﬁcation sections in general. Subjecting
LiBs to heat in excess may induce a rapid thermal runaway reaction, but the heat ﬂux
provided by typical surrounds, may not be enough to produces such an event. The
study [Andrey W. Golubkov and et al., 2013] showed that 150oC was the lowest onset
temperature at which thermal runaway occurred in ordinary consumer batteries. Tests in
that study were performed on single batteries. Investigations on batteries stored in bulk
were not discovered throughout the research performed. From studies on other products
that are prone to thermal runaway reactions, bulk storage tends to lower the critical ambient
temperature. For this reason external heat sources are considered, a potential cause of the
critical event. External ﬁres in adjacent containers could provide the onset temperatures
of 150oC to 210oC that have been shown to produce rapid thermal runaway reactions in
ordinary consumer batteries.
Consequence of critical event in relation to lithium-ion batteries
==PAGE:76==
76
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.18: Diagram showing the initial consequences of thermal runaway in lithium-ion
batteries, part 1
==PAGE:77==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
77
Figure 3.19: Diagram showing the initial consequences of thermal runaway in lithium-ion
batteries, part 2
==PAGE:78==
78
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
With reference to ﬁgure 3.18 and 3.19, initially collapse of the internal structure in the LiB
is considered. If this does not occur it is unlikely that the situation would escalate any further.
If it does occur the next thing considered is whether the cell vents with ﬂame or not, if not it is
considered if the cell leaks electrolytic compound. If this does not occur its unlikely that the
situation could escalate any further. Subsequently it is considered whether the electrolytes are
ignited or not, if not no further escalation seems possible, if it is ignited it’s likely that it will
produce a ﬁre or explosion. Moving back to the decision box considering venting with ﬂame of
one cell. If this is fulﬁlled, it’s considered if any other cells vent with ﬂame. As it seems unlikely
that one cell venting with ﬂame would produce a ﬁre or explosion. Therefore, if no more than
one cell vents with ﬂame it is assumed that no further escalation occur.If more than one cell vents
with ﬂame it seems likely that a chain reaction elapses, igniting surrounding cells. This would
produce an explosion or ﬁre. The next decision box considers if the ﬁre or explosion is detected.
Upon detection an initial response is performed to assess the severity and location of the ﬁre.
Upon returning a ﬁreﬁghting response would be dispatched to suppress the ﬁre. If any of these
three steps aren’t performed or not performed efﬁciently it provides time for the ﬁre to spread to
adjacent containers. This is considered through a loop with three parallel paths. As previous
analysis performed subsequently it is considered whether the SCBA and the SCBA-ﬁlling system
is effective. If not, as the batteries have a potential to produce various combustion product and
hydrogen ﬂuoride, which is very corrosive to the skin and respiratory system. If the SCBA had a
leak ﬁreﬁghters could be severely injured by such products producing casualties. Alternatively a
fault to the ﬁlling system would make it impossible to reﬁll empty air supply bottles. Considering
that the SCBA and systems supporting it performs effectively the next decision box evaluates
the effectiveness of the ﬁreﬁghting response. If this is effective, meaning that the individual
containers affected are extinguished or the ﬁre is kept from spreading, little damage would be
expected. Alternatively the ﬁreﬁghting efforts aren’t effective causing major damage and in
worst case causing the ship to sink.
General frequencies of escalation path - known container ship ﬁres
Through the initial investigation of real-life ﬁre scenarios it was possible to uncover one ﬁre
incident which was initiated as a consequence of thermal runaway in lithium-ion batteries i.e.
the ﬁre incident on the container ship CMA CGM Rossini 15th June 2016. General cause
relations and frequencies of escalation paths can therefore not be drawn, as no comparison
material is available. Instead an isolated comparison between the discovered case and the cause
consequence study conducted for thermal runaway in lithium-ion batteries, was performed.
Through investigation of the actual ﬁre incident elaborated in section 3.1.3 and the attained study,
it was possible reﬂect the actual cause of the ﬁre incident and the following escalation path of
the ﬁre onto the attained cause consequence diagram. The comparison and attained results are
illustrated in ﬁgure 3.20 and 3.21.
