Cause consequence diagram - Divinylbenzene
The chemical properties and potential hazards concerning divinylbezene(DVB) were described
in section 3.2.5 and has formed the basis of developing a cause consequence analysis of said
product. From the identiﬁed hazards the main critical event was identiﬁed as polymerization
of DVB. Potential trajectories that could cause such an event have been presented in the cause
consequence diagram in appendix A.4.4. The the causes and consequences of the critical event
have been presented in the ﬁgure 3.22 and ﬁgure 3.23 and 3.24, respectively.
Critical event & cause of critical event
Figure 3.22: Cause of critical event - Polymerization of divinylbenzene
As the polymerization reaction of DVB produces heat and accelerates with increasing tem-
perature, the polymerization may cause a thermal runaway. The potential causes of such an event
have been established as external heating, contamination and limited available TBC(polymerization
inhibitor).
• External heat source
– Above the weather deck: as with previous cases, placing cargo on the deck comes
with the probability of having the it directly exposed to the sun. From studies this
could cause internal temperatures of approximately 50oC.
– Below the weather deck: a series of machinery and heat sources are present that
could provide a continuous elevated temperature inside a general purpose storage
container. These sources have been accounted for in the cause consequence analysis
on calcium hypochlorite. As established in the hazard identiﬁcation section on DVB,
storage at elevated temperatures would deplete the TBC inhibitor at a much greater
rate than at normal ambient temperature, see table 3.8. As the inhibitor is depleted
the polymerization of DVB monomers will proceed uncontrollably, i.e. causing the
==PAGE:82==
82
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
decided upon critical event.
• External ﬁres: are a source of heat in the same way as machinery and HFO-tanks,
although the effects are more immediate. The heat ﬂux from a ﬁre could increase the
polymerization rate substantially, causing the critical event.
• Contamination: A number of contaminants described in section 3.2.5 have the potential
to cause violent polymerization of DVB. As with calcium hypochlorite, the pathways for
contaminants to enter drums containing the DVB are limited while it is in transit. It is
more likely that such contaminants are introduced through recycled drums or improper
handling of the product prior to shipment.
• Insufﬁcient inhibitor levels: the monomers in DVB would spontaneously polymerize if
the levels of TBC or oxygen were decreased below levels deﬁned in section 3.2.5, leading
to the critical event presented in ﬁgure 3.22. Low inhibitor levels may occur if the product
has not been stored properly or improper quality control at the producer causing a bad
batch to leave the factory. As TBC relies on available oxygen to inhibit polymerization, if
aeration has not been performed prior to shipment the TBC will be rendered useless
Consequence of critical event
==PAGE:83==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
83
Figure 3.23: Consequences of critical event - Polymerization of divinylbenzene part 1
==PAGE:84==
84
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
Figure 3.24: Consequences of critical event - Polymerization of divinylbenzene part 2
==PAGE:85==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
85
The ﬁrst thing considered is whether a thermal runaway reaction occurs; if it doesn’t no
further consequences arise. If the thermal runaway reaction condition is satisﬁed the product
self-heats violently. The temperature will either reach a temperature above or below DVB’s
self ignition temperature. Considering the ﬁrst possibility, limited oxygen is available inside
the canisters/drums therefore no foundation for combustion is available. The second possibility,
that the temperature does not reach the auto ignition temperature, leaves two part of the ﬁre
triangle unfulﬁlled. Either way the next conditional box considers whether the canisters/drums
rupture, if not no further escalation is possible. In the case that the DVB is above the auto
ignition temperature and ruptures, the product will catch ﬁre. In the other case, on rupture
DVB-vapors would ﬁll the shipping container and possibly leak through the vents. As DVB
vapors are explosive in quantities of 0.67 to 6.14% and are heavier than air they may travel a
great distance prior to ignition. Whether or not ignition occurs is considered in the "ignition
occurs" decision box. If not, the probability of ignition would still be a latent hazard, as the
explosive vapors, produced by the volatile DVB, are still present. If ignition of DVB vapors
does take place, due to sparks from e.g. hot work like welding or a ﬂuorescent light breaking
from vibrations, this would cause an explosion or a ﬁre. The next decision box is the same for
either case, it considers whether the ﬁre or explosion is detected or not. If it’s not detected, an
escalation loop steps into place in which the next box considers that the ﬁre spreads to other
shipping containers. Whether it does or doesn’t the loop continuous until the ﬁre or explosion is
detected. Upon detecting the ﬁre/explosion an initial emergency response would be dispatched
to assess the detected occurrence. If this is not possible or nothing is done to investigate the
situation the consequence pathway commences back to the escalation decision box, previously
described. Considering the that the emergency response successfully validates the proximate
location and extent of the ﬁre/explosion the next step is to commence a ﬁreﬁghting response,
as seen in ﬁgure 3.23. If the ﬁre ﬁghting response is not conducted the chain of move on to
establish that ﬁre escalates, as an uncontrolled ﬁre has a high likelihood of spreading, causing
signiﬁcant damage to the content of adjacent shipping containers.
If the ﬁre ﬁghting response is satisﬁed, the next subject considered is whether or not the
SCBA is effective. This both concerns the ﬁlling station purposed with recharging spent air
cylinders and the SCBA’s integrity, i.e. the equipment’s ability to keep out combustion gasses,
like carbon monoxide and carbon dioxide. In a case where the ﬁlling station fails the ﬁre ﬁghting
response would not be possible to continue without causing signiﬁcant human casualties. The
same applies to malfunctioning SCBA equipment. As it wouldn’t be possible to commence
the ﬁre ﬁghting response without causing human casualties, escalation of and spread of ﬁre is
therefore considered the next logical step. For a case in which the SCBA-equipment is effective,
the next step evaluated is the effectiveness of the ﬁre ﬁghting response. Effective ﬁre ﬁghting is
summarizing on a broad ﬁeld of methods for extinguishing and suppressing ﬁres, but this thesis
point of view effective ﬁre ﬁghting covers the ability to hinder the ﬁre from spreading to adjacent
container and/or extinguish the relevant containers. If this isn’t possible the ﬁre ﬁghting response
is considered ineffective and subsequently major damage is expected. In opposite cases limited
damage is expected.
General frequencies of escalation path - known container ship ﬁres
Only one real-life ﬁre scenario was discovered to allegedly have been initiated as a consequence of
the polymerization of divinylbenzene during containerized storage and shipment, namely the ﬁre
incident that occurred on MSC Flaminia the 14th July 2012. Therefore no general cause relations
nor general frequencies of escalation paths can be drawn, based on the available information.
Instead a comparison was conducted between the isolated ﬁre incident that took place on MSC
Flaminia and the diagram attained through the cause consequence study conducted for the
polymerization of divinylbenzene. In this comparison, the authors assumes that the ﬁre incident
==PAGE:86==
86
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
on MSC Flaminia was initiated as a consequence of the polymerization of divinylbenzene, even
though it still remains one of several hypothesizes elaborated in the report issued by the BSU.
The result of the comparison is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.25 and 3.26.
Figure 3.25: Cause consequence diagram for the polymerization of divinylbenzene with included
cause relations and escalation paths for the real-life ﬁre scenario on MSC Flaminia, part 1
==PAGE:87==
3.3 Cause consequence analysis
87
Figure 3.26: Cause consequence diagram for the polymerization of divinylbenzene with included
cause relations and escalation paths for the real-life ﬁre scenario on MSC Flaminia, part 2
==PAGE:88==
88
Chapter 3. Fire Scenarios & Hazard Identiﬁcation
As no ﬁnal conclusion was drawn on the actual cause for the initiation of the ﬁre on MSC
Flaminia, it was not possible to mirror a cause of the critical event. Based on the authors
assumption about the critical event and information attained from the investigation conducted
by the BSU (which is elaborated in section 3.1.4) it was possible to mirror the actual path of
escalation with the consequence chains attained from the study.
It is in these situations, where limited information is available about the high consequence
ﬁre hazards, the capability of the cause consequence method is shown. The reason being that
the cause consequence studies, provides a method which allows safety engineers to predict
or foresee potential causes which could provoke the occurrence of the critical events, which
in this particular case was the polymerization of divinylbenzene. From these studies storage
recommendations, the hazardous nature of the cargo, exposure dangers, handling criteria etc. can
be formulated and applied as a supplement or directly in the already existing IMDG code, in
which rules and regulations have been formulated for the same purpose.
The study further allows, potential consequence chains to be discovered and analyzed. The at-
tained information can help to improve the crews ability to asses ﬁre situations by understanding
the possible escalation paths and ﬁnal events, that may arise as a consequence of the critical
event. Thereby, allowing a better and immediate consequence assessment of the ﬁre, which
improves the probability of achieving a rapid and effective ﬁre ﬁghting response. In hazardous
scenarios where the likelihood of the ﬁre inﬂicting major damages is high, a call for immediate
external assistance could improve the chances of terminating the ﬁre prior realization of the
foreseen ﬁnal event.
==PAGE:89==
4. Accident Frequencies
To determine the overall frequencies at which large cargo ﬁres occur aboard container ships;
historical accidents may be cataloged and put in relation to ship exposure years. The following
chapter aims at determining these from the previously tabulated ﬁres in table 3.1 aboard container
ships put into relation with exposure data collected by the United Nations (UN).
4.1
Methodology
The methodology for determining accident frequencies were obtained from [Spouge, 1999]. To
provide adequately evaluated frequencies the following steps were followed:
• Deﬁne scope of data, meaning that it should be clearly deﬁned what the analysis aims at
analyzing to make sure relevant or matching data is collected.
• Collect accident data, which should be extracted from all available sources. Combining
different sources often reveals the limits or knowledge gaps of one source.
• Screen for data limitations, upon collecting available data it is possible that there are
simply too few accidents to provide a conﬁdent result.
• Collect exposure data, determining installation years or operating years is paramount in
determining accident frequencies from historical data. Otherwise it is not possible to
provide accidents per operating year.
• Calculate accident frequencies and conﬁdence limits, this may simply be to divide number
of accidents by operation time. Good practice advises on considering conﬁdence limits
via chi-squared distributions.
• Validate the frequency estimate, upon determining the frequencies it is advised to com-
pare these with other estimated accident frequencies that may have been determined via
theoretical approaches.
The accident frequencies are determined through Eq. 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3, providing the actual
frequency and conﬁdence limits, respectively.
F = N
Y
(4.1)
, N being the number of accident and Y being the number of exposure years.
==PAGE:90==
90
Chapter 4. Accident Frequencies
Chi-squared conﬁdence limits may be added to the previous equation, providing an estimate
of validity. From recommendations a 90% range is sufﬁcient in qualitative risk analysis. This
gives a 90% chance that the true frequencies lie within the calculated range, but also a 5%
change that it may be either higher or lower, provided that the data, which the frequencies
have been calculated from, have a constant underlying frequency. Not taken account for in the
conﬁdence range is a series of other sources of error like incomplete event data or certain trends
in frequencies.

4.2
Scope of the investigation
Scoping the investigation has to some extent been done throughout the previous sections. To
summarize, only major ﬁres causing down-time in the continuous operation of a container ship
have been considered in the retrieval of data on cargo container ﬁres.
4.3
Accident data
Collected accident data has been presented in table 4.1, and form the basis of determining the
overall frequency of cargo ﬁres aboard container ships.
4.4

publication called "Review of Maritime Transport", which summarizes statistics concerning
the global ﬂeet of ships. This includes an annual count of the number of active container ships.
Historical data from this publication have been presented in table 1.1. A discrepancy is noted
between the available data on number of active ships and available data on cargo ﬁres clearly
visible. UNCTAD did no t register the number of ships before 2011 making collected data on
cargo ﬁre prior to this non-relevant to this study. Also, accident data from 2017 isn’t considered
as the year hasn’t ended yet.

4.6
Evaluation of frequencies
Considering the results in table 4.2 the best estimate of ﬁres is 6.86·10−4 per ship year. There
is a 90% chance that actual frequency lies within the interval 4.56·10−4 and 9.94·10−4. This
estimate may not be entirely unreasonable, i.e. most cases of cargo ﬁres have been discovered,
as the NORSOK Z-013 puts forward an acceptability criteria of 1·10−4 per accident load. The
proportions are therefore in the same order. Objectively if the frequency of cargo ﬁres occurring
aboard container ships is higher than estimated, the incentive for decreasing these or the severity
of these would be even greater. The frequency could be utilized in performing an ALARP
analysis, to evaluate potential solutions for risk reducing measures.
==PAGE:92==
5. Detection
The aim of this section is to explore and uncover a solution space of ideas or preliminary concepts,
which could potentiate the ﬁrst safety barrier in the protection against ﬁres, namely ﬁre detection.
To do so a collaborative, investigative and innovative idea development process was conducted.
The overall aim of the attained solution space was to uncover and establish a foundation for
creating a number of realistic design concepts, from which when realized exiting and innovative
ﬁre detection products could be developed. All in the search of improving the overall ﬁre safety
aboard container ships.
The reader will throughout the following sections be introduced to the investigatory process
at which the solution space was developed. Further, an introduction to the technologies which
create the basis for the developed solution space, the solution idea itself and a validation study
will be presented, for two of the attained solutions.
5.1
Silent brainstorming
In order to investigate and create a solution space of ideas for improving the detection arrange-
ments aboard container ships, a brainstorming process was conducted. The brainstorming
process was performed through collaborative sessions between the authors of this thesis and J.
Robert Taylor. A number of different approaches can be taken when executing a brainstorming
process. The three main approaches for brainstorming are unstructured-, structured- and silent
brainstorming. Each of the different approaches will be elaborated in below methodology section.
5.1.1
Methodology
Brainstorming can be conducted silently, structured, unstructured or as a combination of the
latter 2, [Ofﬁce, 2017]. It is a useful tool in the initial phase of a risk assessment and especially
when analysing system possible component failures and operational hazards. The main aim of
brainstorming is to unravel or illuminate a certain subject, in regards to component failures the
purpose could be to identify vulnerable relations in e.g. sensor-feedback loops. To produce good
results during a brainstorm an emphasis should be put on including qualiﬁed people.
Unstructured brainstorming: The most well-known form of brainstorming is the un-
structured form. Prior to the session the invited group is provided with the purpose of the
==PAGE:93==
5.2 Solution space
93
brainstorming and relevant drawings and diagrams. The format of a session is to place a group of
competent people at a table and ask them to generate ideas on the subject. A present moderator
will write up the ideas on a whiteboard. Here, it is important to be tolerant of each others ideas
and not criticize. Everyone is allowed to and should pitch in. The advantage this is a relaxed/free
environment which can prove very beneﬁcial for the quality of the end product and people
are able to build on each others ideas. The free environment may however cause some of the
participants to ﬁnd it difﬁcult to take part.
Structured brainstorming: A structured brainstorming works on the same principles as the
free formed. To provide structure, the sessions moderator initializes the session by letting one of
the participants provide their immediate response to the subject at hand. The person to the left or
right does the same and so on. As the sequence progresses people are allowed to pass if they do
not have any ideas. If at any point the whole group passes, the session is over. The advantage
of structured brainstorming is that every person has an equal chance to participate in the idea
generation. The disadvantage is the lack of spontaneity, which may to some extent defeat the
purpose of the free format behind the brainstorming session.
Silent brainstorming: People are presented with the problem by a moderator, like the
previous to cases. Instead of the plenary sharing of ideas, every participant is presented with
post-it notes or slips of papers. They are then tasked with writing up ideas on the subject
presented to them. To conclude on the session, the notes are gathered and placed on a whiteboard
to give the participants and the moderator an overview. These ideas are then presented in plenum
to give the participants an opportunity to elaborate on their thoughts and generate more ideas.
5.1.2
Silent brainstorming process
The approach which was considered most sufﬁcient for taking advantage of each of the par-
ticipants individual knowledge about the problem at hand and thereby attain the best possible
solution space was the silent brainstorming. The process was initialized by appointing one of
the participants as the moderator of the brainstorming session. Mr. Taylor was assigned the task
as session moderator, to allow the authors to focus on exploring the solution space. Besides
applying the knowledge already attained through the scientiﬁc endeavor of this thesis, a broad
information and extensive market search was conducted in preparation of this session combined
with collaborative discussions in order to create the most qualiﬁed basis for the brainstorm. Each
of the participants were subsequently handed a note pad, for silently working out individual
ideas for potential solutions. The individual solution process lasted approximately 45 minutes,
allowing the participants to generate a sufﬁcient number of ideas, individually considered satis-
factory for the solution space.
Following the individual solution generation process, the moderator structured a presentation
process. The intend of the presentation process was to allow each participant to sequentially
present one idea in plenum and further provide the opportunity to elaborate on the thoughts
behind the solution without interruption. In parallel with this process a combined overview of
all the presented ideas of solutions were handled and noted by the moderator, in order to catch
and separate colliding ideas/solutions. The individual presentation of all the developed solutions
lasted approximately 1 hour. By the end of the silent brainstorming a solution space containing 7
ideas of solutions for improving the detection methods aboard container ships were obtained.

The solution space attained through the process of the silent brainstorming are presented in table
5.1. The table of solutions contains a short description of the solution technology and further the

Only two solutions will be investigated in the following paragraphs as these were deemed
most relevant for improving ﬁre detection aboard container ships, based on immediate validation.
The following sections will focus on the technologies which creates the basis for the developed
solutions, the solution idea itself and a validation of the explored solutions.
5.2.1
Solution No. 1 - temperature sensor
The idea of placing temperature sensors in or on all shipping containers was proposed during
the silent brainstorming. To investigate the possibilities of placing temperature sensors in or on
shipping containers the technologies behind measuring temperature will brieﬂy be described.
Further, the greater picture describing application of the idea will be presented.
Technology description and solution application
Temperature sensors utilize a series of technologies to estimate ambient temperature. The
following technologies are widely applied throughout systems utilizing temperature sensors
for regulating processes, knowledge hereof was acquired through RS-Online, a distributor of
electronic merchandise:
• Resistance temperature detectors, or RTD’s essentially take advantage of materials like
platinum, nickel and copper’s electrical resistance and that this changes with temperature.
The change in resistance for most of these materials change linearly with increase in
temperature. The accuracy is among the best of all types of sensors, but while measuring
the power consumption is also relatively high compared to alternative sensor technologies.
The measurement range is typically between −250oC and 750oC.
• Thermocouples, take advantage of the temperature dependent amount of voltage produced
when different metals are joined together. This is called a thermoelectric effect. Different
combinations of metals produce different responses for temperature change. A quite
common combination is iron and a copper-nickel alloy called constantan. This combination
provides a sensitivity of 50µV/oC and an operating range of −40oC to 750oC.
• Integrated circuit temperature sensor, or IC Sensors are widely applied in consumer
electronics as they are cheap, have low power consumption and provide reasonable
accurate temperature measurements within −55oC and 150oC. The sensor packaging has
a low thermal mass meaning the response time to temperature change is quite fast relative
==PAGE:95==
5.2 Solution space
95
to other types of sensors. Further, these types of sensors can be acquired with analog
to digital converters(ADC) built directly into them, meaning that transfer of the sensed
temperature can easily be communicated to an external device without the need for an
external analog signal converter.
Considering the available technologies to measure ambient temperature, the IC-sensor type
seems like the most obvious choice. This on the basis that the temperature span they are able to
measure within is sufﬁcient, considering the research provided in the hazard identiﬁcation study.
As the IC-sensors have built-in ADC a setup would require limited separate components. Inside
the container there should therefore only be a power source(battery), IC-sensor and a wireless
transmitter.
Figure 5.1: Diagram describing the concept of installing temperature sensors in each container
that communicates with a central control station
As of such the main idea would be to place a temperature sensor inside or on the outside
of every shipping container. These would register temperature changes inside the space of the
shipping container either directly inside the container or via heat transfer through the casing of
the container. This has been depicted in ﬁgure 5.1.
To avoid making the crew having to deal with connecting data cables on every transfer
of containers the sensors would have to be able to communicate via a wireless connection to
central receivers located throughout the ship. The temperature sensor would simply transmit
the present temperature and the containers serial number. These would then answer to a central
control station evaluating changes in temperature relative to the ambient temperature of the cargo
space or outdoors temperature. The serial number could be compared to the locations of each
container available in the loading computer. With an internal or external temperature sensor a
thermal runaway would be possible to detect prior to the internal material igniting or the reaction
becoming uncontrollable. All-in-all the crew would be provided with an alert of an affected
container and its location.
Both the sensor and wireless transmitter would require a power source. A battery is the
obvious choice in providing this. Depending on the power consumption of such a setup it
could require charging or preferably not withing the 5 year servicing cycle containers undergo.
Charging could however be achieved though an inductive charging coil placed on land or similar.
==PAGE:96==
96
Chapter 5. Detection

the overall pros and cons should be considered for doing so. These were formulated to assess
the feasibility of the proposed solutions in a structured manor. Additionally action points were
established to determine necessary further steps required to validate the effectiveness of the
proposed concepts. In table 5.2 the validation scheme attained based on the validation study
conducted for the application of temperature sensors is presented.

==PAGE:98==

Chapter 5. Detection
Advantages and disadvantages - Internally mounted temperature sensors
From the session discussing the advantages and disadvantages of mounting temperature sensors
in every shipping container especially the idea of it having a fast response time was decided upon
as a major advantage. It was established in plenum that e.g. a runaway reaction or similar would
be detected faster than through other methods, as such a reaction would produce signiﬁcant
quantities of heat prior to ignition.
The shear amount of temperature sensors to be installed is the main disadvantage along with
standardization. Expenditures from purchasing a IC-temperature sensor may be in the range of
3-4$, along with a wireless transmitter at 4-5$ and a battery in the same price range and putting
these together may produce a product in the 20$ range. However as a ULCS carry upwards of
20,000 TEU (predominantly 40 ft. containers), more than 10,000 sensor setup would be needed
for one installation. The global ﬂeet of container ships currently include 5,154 ships, making the
quantity of sensors required to be installed globally enormous. In addition to this, standards must
be in place so that the equipment aboard a Maersk vessel is compatible with a MSC vessel and
so on. Such challenges are usually resolved in within the ISO, but the prospects may be long,
depending differences among stakeholders.
Secondarily as the temperature sensor device would provide the serial number of the affected
container, the location of the container could be identiﬁed. Locating the affected container,
especially under the weather deck, is often a major challenge as explained by Øssur Hildenburg
in the interview provided in appendix A.1. Providing an exact location of the affected container
would make this task considerably easier, why it was considered an advantage.
As with virtually any equipment installed on a vessel, the temperature sensor device would
require occasional maintenance. This is not an issue in it self, but the responsibility of performing
the maintenance is necessary place at some actor. This should preferably be the owner of
the container, who is also responsible for maintaining the requirements set out to uphold the
CSC-plate. Such a responsibility would increase the operational expenditures of transporting
containers.
Robustness of the sensor device and supplying it with power, are two relevant technical
difﬁculties. From the interview with Øssur Hildenburg provided in appendix A.1, previous
attempts at placing sensors in the container proved ineffective due to damages inﬂicted on the
equipment through use of the containers. This is of course a challenge, but it may be solved
through clever design and environmental tests of the equipment. Lithium based batteries for
e.g. smoke detectors that have a 10 year functionality warranty, are available. Thus, batteries
able to supply such equipment with power for 5 years or more may be feasible. Alternatively an
inductive charging unit could be included in the device, allowing for external charging without
or with limited crew interaction.
False alarms produced by optical based smoke detectors are not uncommon. These may
be activated by dust or water vapor which temperature sensors are not susceptible to. Thus
alarms from temperature-based detection would provide a more valid indication of a potentially
hazardous situation.
Advantages and disadvantages - Externally mounted temperature sensors
Installing temperature sensors on the outside of the container have the same disadvantages as
installing the sensor device on the inside of the container. Except eventual maintenance may
be feasible while the shipping container is carrying cargo, which would be relevant giving a
malfunction during transit.
The advantages of placing the sensor device on the outside of the container, may be less
potent than the above alternative. As heat given of by a potential runaway reaction or similar
would have to transfer through the container casing for it to be detected, the response would be
inhibited compared to internal temperature measurements.
==PAGE:99==
5.2 Solution space
99
Localization of the affected container could be impaired by external sources of heat from
ﬁres below or adjacent to a sensor device. This would make alarms provided by the sensor more
indicative of the relevant location, compared to internal placement of the sensor.
Action point relevant to temperature sensors
In order to validate the effectiveness of this solution, a study should be performed to investigate
the response time compared to e.g. sample extraction smoke detection systems.
5.2.2
Solution No. 3 - Technology description of smoke detectors
Smoke detection is one of the most common technologies applied for the detection of ﬁres and
are widely used in industries as well as private homes. Smoke detectors are already a part of the
regulatory framework on ﬁre safety aboard container ships. The extent to which they are used, is
considered possible to improve.
Various types of smoke detectors currently exist on the market, but they all share one fea-
ture in detection, namely than they rely on the emission of smoke for the detection of ﬁres. The
most common smoke detection technology and the one considered for this solution is the optical
smoke detector. An example of an optical smoke detector is presented in ﬁgure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: Photo of optical smoke detector
Source: www.ukew.co.uk
Technology description and solution application
The main components of an optical smoke detector are;
• A light proof chamber cover
• Infra-red LED
• Photodiode light receptor
• Opening vents and insect intercepting screens (to prevent false alarms caused by a bug
entering the smoke detector chamber).
The infra-red LED emits a beam of light into the sensor chamber covered and created by the
light proof chamber cover. This happens every 10 seconds to register if any smoke particles
have entered the chamber. At the point of ﬁre, smoke will enter the chamber through the vents.
The smoke particles will then cause the infra-red light to be reﬂected onto the photodiode light
receptor. When this happens a signal is sent to the integrated circuit which then initiates the alarm
system. Information on smoke detector technologies was obtained via Safelincs Ltd. website.
The intended use of the optical smoke detector, for this solution, is to install one in each
container. This would allow smoke to be detected almost immediately after being dispersed
from a ﬁre igniting within one of the sealed containers. The reason for installing them in all
==PAGE:100==
100
Chapter 5. Detection
containers instead of just those stored with dangerous goods, is the concern previously discussed
about the likelihood of transporting mis-declared dangerous goods. A diagram depicting such a
solution is available in ﬁgure 5.3 where the smoke detector is located in the top right corner of
the container. The smoke detector would produce a wireless signal if smoke is detected, stating
the serial number of the affected container, allowing for rational localization via the loading
computer.
Figure 5.3: Diagram describing the concept of installing smoke detectors in each container that
communicates with a central control station
Solution validation
As previous a validation process of the proposed solution was performed in plenum, while
documenting the results hereof in table 5.3. Considered was the pros and cons of implementing
smoke detectors in every container, as well as relevant action points that needed investigation to
assess the effectiveness of such a solution.

Advantages and disadvantages - Internally located smoke detectors
Like the internally mounted temperature sensors, the smoke detectors would provide a fast
response if a ﬁre develops inside a container. Compared to the temperature sensor the smoke
detectors would however not provide an indication of a ﬁre before the material is ignited or
it produces fumes that may ignite. The response time would however be faster than a sample
extraction smoke detection system.
The other advantages are equal to those covered in the section describing the temperature
sensor device. This applies to the disadvantages as well. Aside from the possibility of false
alarms, which optical smoke detectors are prone to producing if subjected to dust or water vapor.
Action point relevant to internally mounted smoke detectors
In order to validate the effectiveness of this solution, a study should be performed to investigate
the response time compared to e.g. sample extraction smoke detection systems.
==PAGE:103==
6. Fire Development
The development of ﬁre is created through a chemical reaction between fuel, oxygen and heat.
Figure 6.1: Fire triangle
The fuel represents combustible materials, which can exist in three states of matter: gas,
liquid and solid. Speciﬁc for containerized shipment, the fuel exists through the cargo stored
within the containers. Even though these fuels can vary through their chemical and physical
states, they all share features in ﬁre. The differences between the fuels are most clearly seen in
their process of initiation (ignition), how fast they can develop (ﬁre growth) and the power the ﬁre
is able to generate. By trying to understand the science of ﬁres, the ability to quantify and predict
the behavior of ﬁres can be improved [Drysdale, 2016]. This knowledge can subsequently be
applied in the process of improving general ﬁre safety, or in this case the ﬁre safety aboard
container ships.
6.1
Basic concepts and main processes in ﬁre development
This section will serve as an introductory to the main processes in ﬁre development, with the
intend of providing a fundamental understanding of ﬁres.
6.1.1
Heat transfer
In order to understand the behavior of ﬁre or a ﬁre development process, one initially needs to
understand the concept of heat transfer. Heat transfer deﬁnes the rate at which heat is transferred
from one object to another. Heat always transfers from an hotter object to a cooler one. When
explaining heat transfer, three phenomena are worth mentioning: Conduction, convection and
==PAGE:104==
104
Chapter 6. Fire Development
radiation. Each of which will be presented below. The theory presented in the sections below
has been acquired through [NIST, 2017] and [Drysdale, 2016] .
Conduction:
Thermal conduction deﬁnes the transfer of heat through solids.
Figure 6.2: Illustration of conduction
Conduction can be calculated by applying equation 6.1.
˙q = Ak
l (Thot −Tcold)
(6.1)
Where A is the area exposed to the heat [m2], k is the thermal conductivity [kW/mK], l is the
thickness of the solid and T the temperature difference from Thot to Tcold[K].
Convection:
Convection describes the transfer of heat from a moving ﬂuid to a solid.
Figure 6.3: Illustration of convection
Convection can be calculated through equation 6.2.
˙q = h(Thot −Tcold)A
(6.2)
Where h deﬁnes the convective heat transfer coefﬁcient of the process and is unique for
different fuels[kW/m2K], T is again the temperature difference from Thot to Tcold[K] and A the
area exposed to the heat [m2].
Radiation:
Radiation requires no medium to transfer heat. The heat is transferred via electromagnetic waves.
The waves delivers heat energy to the surfaces that are subject to these.
==PAGE:105==
6.1 Basic concepts and main processes in ﬁre development
105
Figure 6.4: Illustration of radiation
The radiation magnitude can be measure by applying equation 6.3.
˙q = (εαT 4
hot)A
(6.3)
Where ε deﬁnes the ability of the fuels surface to emit energy by radiation also known as
emissivity, α the speed at which the fuel is able to adjust its temperature to the surroundings
known as thermal diffusivity [m2/s], T the temperature [K] and A the size of the area exposed to
the radiation [m2].
6.1.2
Burning rate and heat release rate
The burning rate expresses the mass of fuels consumed by the combustion process over time
[g/s]. Equation 6.4 can be applied to describe this, for solid and liquid fuels.
˙m =
˙
Q′′
F −˙Q′′
L
LV
A fuel
(6.4)
Where ˙
Q′′
F describes the rate of heat energy transferred from the ﬂame to the surface [kW/m2],
˙Q′′
L is the rate of heat loss from the surface [kW/m2], LV is the heat required to turn the fuel into
ﬂammable gasses which is equal to the energy required to evaporate the fuel [kJ/g] and A fuel is
the surface area of the fuel [m2].
Heat release rate (HRR) is a measure for the amount of energy released from mass burned.
The HRR is a single parameter which is used to determine the ﬁre behavior of a material. The
HRR can be calculated by applying equation 6.5 and is measured in kilo joules pr. second [kJ/s]
or kilowatt [kW].
HRR = ˙m∆HC
(6.5)
Where ∆HC is equivalent to the effective heat of combustion of the fuel [kJ/g].
6.1.3
Fire development process
The development of ﬁre can be categorized into different stages, as illustrates in ﬁgure 6.5.
==PAGE:106==
106
Chapter 6. Fire Development
Figure 6.5: Illustration the ﬁre development process
An introduction to the different stages will be elaborated in the following sections.
Ignition
Fire ignition describes the instant at which a ﬁre initiates. Ignition occurs when the temperature
of a fuel reaches a level (ﬂashpoint) sufﬁcient to ignite the build up vapours, given a speciﬁc
ignition source.
Figure 6.6: Ignition phase
The point of ignition is highly depended on the fuel (Some fuels might be able to self-
ignite under some circumstances) and the ignition source, which is also mirrored in the cause
consequence studies conducted in Chapter 3. Some potential ignition sources are listed in table
6.1.
==PAGE:107==
6.1 Basic concepts and main processes in ﬁre development
107
Table 6.1: Potential ignition sources
Source
Ignition examples
Hot work
Can produce enough heat or sparks which would be a potential ignition source
Hot surfaces
If the hot surface exceeds the minimum self ignition temperature of speciﬁc fuels
Friction
Extensive friction between some objects can cause the development
of mechanical sparks which could cause ignition
Electrical equipment
The shattering of a light-bulb or a ﬂuorescent tube could
cause an igniting spark
Chemical reaction
The incorrect storage of hazardous chemicals could cause self ignition
Cigarette ember
The ember of a lighted cigarette could ignite a ﬁre
UV
Ignition caused by UV and IR radiation (e.g. from the sun)
Shock
Ignition caused by shock energy
Growth
Followed the ignition of a ﬁre there will be a slow or rapid ﬁre progression which constitutes the
growth of the ﬁre.
Figure 6.7: Growth phase
Generally when focusing on real-life container ﬁre scenarios, very little is known about this
phase, since most of the evidence is lost in the ﬁre. The rate at which a ﬁre progresses can vary
depending on a number of factors. Some of the most important ones are the fuel which caused
the initiation of the ﬁre (fuel properties), the fuel quantity, the available oxygen (ventilation,
compartment geometry etc.) and the adjacent fuels (if any).
==PAGE:108==
108
Chapter 6. Fire Development
Flash-over and fully developed ﬁre
The most dangerous stage of a developing enclosed ﬁre is the ﬂashover. Flashover is the
phenomenon in which a complete enclosure becomes involved in the ﬁre.
Figure 6.8: ﬂashover/fully developed ﬁre phase
A ﬂashover generally occurs when the smoke layer reaches a temperature in excess of 6000C,
thereby causing secondary thermal radiative ignition of adjacent fuels almost simultaneously. It
needs to be noted that ﬂashover does not always occur as it is depends on the ﬁre type. At the
point just subsequent to the ﬂashover all the available fuels are burning, which constitutes the
process of a fully developed ﬁre. During this stage the ﬁre will either reach its highest HRR or
the oxygen is depleted causing the energy release of the ﬁre to decrease.
Decay
As the fuel begins to burn away or the depletion of oxygen decreases the energy of the ﬁre the
process of decay initiates.
Figure 6.9: Decay phase
When the ﬁre reaches the point where one of the three elements in the ﬁre triangle disappears,
the ﬁre will terminate thereby ending the ﬁre process, a stage which is called extinction.
==PAGE:109==
6.2 Fire simulation study using FDS
109
6.2
Fire simulation study using FDS
To explore the development of ﬁres igniting within a sealed 40ft general purpose container, a
ﬁre simulation study was conducted through the CFD program Fire Dynamics Simulator (FDS) .
The main objective of this study, was to investigate smoke dispersion and temperature increase
during a cargo container ﬁre. This study will therefore provide a basis for validating the attained
detection solutions explored in chapter 5.
6.2.1
Modeling methodology
FDS and Smokeview are two separate computational tools developed through a collaborative
endeavor led by the National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) and VTT Technical
Research Centre of Finland. The programs were publicly release in Feburary 2000 and provides
advanced scientiﬁc software for both simulating and visualizing ﬁre dynamics and combustion
in order to analyze and solve practical ﬁre problems to enhance ﬁre protection engineering.
FDS and Smokeview are both open source programs which are continuously being maintained,
updated and improved based on user-feedback and the work carried out by the collaborative
partners[Kevin McGrattan, 2017]. The following sections provides an introduction to the major
features and applications of both FDS and Smokeview.
Fire Dynamics simulator
FDS is a computational ﬂuid dynamics (CFD) model which apply mathematical modeling and
numerical analysis to predict and simulate ﬁre-driven ﬂuid ﬂow.
The model is a numerical solver of Navier-Stokes equations that has been modiﬁed to accom-
modate low-speed (Ma<0.3) thermally driven ﬂows. The underlying algorithms have been
optimized for predicting heat transport and smoke dissipation. The three conservation laws;
conservation of mass, momentum and energy are applied, where the partial derivatives of the
respective conservation equations are approximated as ﬁnite differences and where the solution
is updated in time on a three-dimensional, rectilinear grid. To compute thermal radiation a ﬁnite
volume technique is used applying the same grid as the ﬂow solver. Smoke movement, sprinkler
discharge and fuel sprays are simulated by applying Lagrangian particles. The general and more
basic applications of the model is used to simulate [Gissi, July]:
• Low speed transport of heat and combustion products from ﬁre
• Radiative and convective heat transfer between the gas and solid surfaces
• Smoke generation and dispersion
• Sprinkler, heat detector, and smoke detector activation.
For more advanced engineering applications, FDS can be applied to model;
• Pyrolysis
• Flame spread and ﬁre growth
• Sprinkler sprays and suppression by water.
Smokeview
Smokeview is a visualization program developed to generate images and animations based on
the model results attained by the FDS [Gissi, July].
6.3
Description of initial model
Applying FDS and initiating a ﬁre simulation study is a challenging process, even though a lot of
documentation of the program and well written user manuals are available. The reason being
the enormous wealth of possibilities in the FDS scripting language, the number of parameters
that can be adjusted and the number of parameters that can interact. It is doubtful that anyone
understands the full extent of the programs capabilities.
==PAGE:110==
110
Chapter 6. Fire Development
The following model has been developed in collaboration with risk analyst J. Robert Tay-
lor. Mr. Taylor has extensive experience in FDS programming and has conducted many ﬁre
simulations through his time as a risk analyst.
The model concerns the combustion of cartons of packages of charcoal stored within a general
purpose cargo container. Through this section, the reader will be introduced to the development
and debugging process of the model and model revisions. Further the attained results, the quality
of the simulation and the implications of the results will be discussed.
Development of the FDS model
The modeling process was initialized by reviewing an already existing model of a ﬁre in an
enclosure provided and developed by Mr. Taylor. This model was meant to serve as a basis for
the sought ﬁre simulation study, with the intention of developing the new model by modifying
the original one. It is an approach generally recommended by the developers of FDS and Mr.
Taylor. The reason for this approach is that the possibility of creating early errors are high,
especially when developing a completely new model. Further the debugging process of such
simulations are very hard and time consuming due to the fact that the program is only able to
prompt the user of one error message per script compile. These can also be difﬁcult to intrepid,
adding to the problem of solving present issues. The ﬁnal script used in running the model has
been placed in appendix A.5.
Figure 6.10: View of the original model
The original model was subsequently cleaned up, and modiﬁed to mirror the basic framework
of the general purpose cargo container applicable for the sought simulation. The revised model
can be seen in ﬁgure 6.11.
==PAGE:111==
6.3 Description of initial model
111
Figure 6.11: View of the revised model geometry - initial version
Cargo deﬁnition
In order to simulate the cargo stored within the container for the analysis, the geometry of a
single single cardboard carton ﬁlled with charcoal was initially added, along with the material
and surface for the carton. The parameters for the substances were obtained from SFPE Hand-
book of Fire Protection Engineering [Hurley and et al., 2008] and from Karlsson and Quintierre
Enclosure Fire Dynamics [Bjorn Karlsson, 1999].
Fire initiator
The ﬁre source, i.e. initiator, for this simulation was deﬁned in the form of a pool of heptane,
positioned on top of a small box, the reason being that the ignition of cardboard was deﬁned by
its ignition temperature, so that the gradual growth of the ﬁre could be followed as the cardboard
surfaces became hotter. The ignition of a heptane pool happens immediately following the
mixture combustion approach, except if an ignition temperature or a reaction is speciﬁed for it.
The source of ignition can be deﬁned through other approaches e.g. deﬁning a vent with a high
HRR per unit area or providing some high temperature particles, but applying a small ﬁre close
to the ﬂammable surface as an igniter were found to be a reliable approach.
Mesh deﬁnition
For creating the mesh, a cell size of 0.1 m was intended for the actual calculations. This was
chosen as a ﬁre ball of 10 cm in diameter was considered reasonable as part of a ﬁre which
eventually would emerge into several meters in diameter. But in order to secure a rapid testing
phase of the model, a very coarse mesh was initially deﬁned with a cell size of 0.5·0.5·0.5m3,
thereby limiting the computational time in between debugging. By initially running the program
for a short period of time and subsequently displaying the simulation with smokeview the
geometry of the container and cardboard box appeared okay. But the deﬁned ﬁre source failed to
appear. Upon reﬂection of the failure, the reason was clear. The pool of heptane was deﬁne with
a 0.1m·0.2m in size. As FDS round this to the nearest cell size i.e. 0.0m·0.0m when the mesh
cell is 0.5m·0.5m·0.5m, the ﬁre source failed to appear in the simulation.
Solving bugs and initial test-simulations
After revising the model and running the simulation again, another issue was detected. The
cardboard failed to ignite. It was considered if the heptane igniter was inadequate. To investigate
==PAGE:112==
112
Chapter 6. Fire Development
the reason for the problem, boundary function statements for GAUGE HEAT FLUX and WALL
TEMPERATURE were added to the instrumentation section and the program was rerun.
Through inspection, it was discovered that the temperature of the cardboard did not reach
the speciﬁed 560oC after 120 seconds of simulation time. Therefore the ignition temperature for
cardboard was reduced to 420oC and the program was run again.
After rerunning the simulation, the cardboard seemed to ignite, but the temperatures were
still unchanged, so more instrumentation was added including temperature slices. Also, the
burn-away ﬂag for the cardboard box was set in order to see whether the ﬁre was running out of
fuel. To mimic the conditions of an actual container and ensure a sufﬁcient oxygen supply, vents
were added at the top of its sides:
Added vents:
&HOLE XB=2.0, 2.2, 2.34,2.44, 2.6, 2.7, /
&HOLE XB=6.0, 6.2, 2.34,2.44, 2.6, 2.7, /
&HOLE XB=10.0, 10.2, 2.34,2.44, 2.6, 2.7, /
&HOLE XB=2.0, 2.2, 0.00,0.10, 2.6, 2.7, /
&HOLE XB=6.0, 6.2, 0.00,0.10, 2.6, 2.7, /
&HOLE XB=10.0, 10.2, 0.00,0.10, 2.6, 2.7, /
However, it was detected that the temperature still dropped after about 15 seconds on the
temperature slice above the igniter. It was considered if the ﬁre was running out of oxygen.
In order to secure that the ﬁre received an sufﬁcient oxygen supply, one side of the container
was removed. This had no effect on the ﬁre as the temperature continued to drop after 20
seconds. It was discovered through inspection that the cardboard deﬁned with a wall thickness
of 3 mm, was burning away which caused the temperature drop. To prove this the container
7 model was run, where the wall thickness of the cardboard was deﬁned with 2 cm wall thickness.
The container 8 model was allowed to commence for 30 minutes simulation time. It was
seen in ﬁgure 6.12 that the ﬁre burned down, with just half of the cardboard box burned down.
Again it was considered if the ﬁre was running out of oxygen.
Figure 6.12: Cardboard box burn away
==PAGE:113==
6.3 Description of initial model
113
Instrumentation and further adjustments
The container 9 model was made with CO and oxygen instrumentation (slices) which was added
along the center line of the model. In the container 10 model the wall material for the container
was changed to 3 mm steel. Which made it possible to follow the wall heat up. For container
11, the use of a combustion model was tried instead of specifying heat release rate per unit
area (HRRPUA). The reason was the obvious limitation of a HRRPUA model for charcoal
combustion. Charcoal burns at the solid surface through advection of air and diffusion of oxygen
to the surface through the boundary layer. The HRR will fall when the oxygen level falls. Initially
the model failed as FDS6 only allows one REAC statement per model. Therefore REAC for
heptane was deleted. This was not needed anyway, since HRRPUA for heptane is speciﬁed in
the ignition model.
In container 14 model, additional boxes was added to complete one row. The reason for only
adding one row, was to insure visibility of the burning surfaces. Further two wooden sections
for each box were added in order to represent pallets. For container 15 model the geometry
for the last column of boxes was corrected by changing the box width. A general clean up of
the model was conducted for container 16 model. Soot and CO detectors were implemented
at the vents. In parallel container 14 model, was run again with a long duration calculation to
investigate the general performance of the modl. By displaying the simulation one could see a
ﬂashover/backdraft effect at 95 seconds, with rapid combustion throughout the upper level of the
vessel, see ﬁgure 6.13. Presumably this is soot combustion, because CO concentration was still
considered low. The oxygen concentration was considerably reduced after the ﬂashover, which
can be seen from ﬁgure 6.14.
Figure 6.13: The ﬂashover
==PAGE:114==
114
Chapter 6. Fire Development
Figure 6.14: Oxygen depletion
Investigation of soot ﬂash-over hypothesis
A container 14a model, was created identical to container 14, except that the soot yield of
charcoal was set down to 0.001 in order to evaluate hypothesis on soot combustion in container
14 model.
Still a sudden ﬂashover in the ﬁre appeared, at 55.7 seconds. In retrospect, the experiment was
doomed to failure because the main ﬁre source was by this time the pallets.
Concluding steps
In container 18 model, wind was added. The wind ﬂow can be seen in ﬁgure 6.15. This resulted
in extensive burning at the vents, with internal vent ﬁre on the upwind side and external ﬁre jets
on the downwind side, illustrated in ﬁgure 6.16.
==PAGE:115==
6.3 Description of initial model
115
Figure 6.15: Wind ﬂow around the container
Figure 6.16: Flame jets, driven by wind entering the vents
For container 19 model, the resolution of the mesh was increased to 5 cm, i.e. eight fold
increase in the number of cells. As expected this resulted in much longer run time (by about a
factor 10 increase in times). The physical pattern of combustion was almost unchanged, with very
similar temperature and oxygen concentration proﬁles. But the time for the different combustion
stages was delayed, from about 10 seconds in the ﬁrst minute about 15 seconds after 2 minutes
simulated time.

Non ventilated general purpose cargo container
A new simulation was generated using a non ventilated general purpose cargo container. The
simulation terminated at 55 seconds as the pressure rose very quickly at ﬂashover. A graph
illustrating the pressure development attained through the simulation is presented in ﬁgure 6.23.
Figure 6.23: Pressure development in the non ventilated general purpose cargo container
In this case simulating extensive pressure increases was not possible in FDS. The simulation
terminated due to the this fact, and may caused by FDS’s inability to solve complex compressible
ﬂuid ﬂows. If this had been possible, it might have been possible to evaluated whether the
sudden pressure increase might have ruptured the container. Figures showing oxygen and carbon
dioxide mass fractions 55.7 s into the simulation has been presented in Figure 6.24 and 6.25.
For the oxygen mass fraction plot, orange indicates atmospheric oxygen levels of 21% and dark
blue indicates 9%. In the carbon dioxide mass fraction plot, red indicates 5.56% CO2 and blue
indicates 0.05%.

6.3.1
Observations and comments
From the outset it should be noted that the simulations reported here do not represent an accurate
assessment of ﬁre development in a container. Considerable further research is needed before the
calculations could be relied on with conﬁdence. The direct objective of the simulations were to
support understanding of some aspects of the ﬁre development in boxes of charcoal in a container.
The main uncertainties arise from the dimensions and physical state of the fuel, and the HRR
estimates. Especially the reduction in burning rate after oxygen depletion could not be modeled
here.One direct inaccuracy in the models is that the vent sizes are too large.
==PAGE:121==
6.4 Description of solution validation model
121
Nevertheless several observations can be made which seem sufﬁciently well supported:
1. Once the heat in smoldering charcoal is sufﬁcient to ignite the cardboard box, the develop-
ment of the ﬁre will be rapid, taking just a few minutes until the ﬁre is fully evolved in the
container.
2. The cardboard box and the pallets are the main drivers in development of the ﬁre in the
ﬁrst few minutes.
3. A phenomenon which is a combination of ﬂashover and backdraft occurs once there is
sufﬁcient soot in the container and the upper layer temperature has become sufﬁciently
high. Most of the soot remains captive in the container and it is hypothesized that the
soot from the early stages of the ﬁre mixes with cooler air and then forms a fuel once it is
sufﬁciently heated.
4. The ﬁre begins quickly to become starved of oxygen, especially after ﬂashover.
5. Burning continues at a much reduced rate after oxygen depletion, resulting in decreased
internal temperatures at the ﬁre location.
6. Burning continues at the vents, and can be damaging.
6.4
Description of solution validation model
With reference to the action points described in section 5.2.1 and 5.2.2 a model was developed
to investigate the response time of monitoring the temperature and smoke dispersion in a 40 ft.
general purpose shipping container. The model utilized for testing the described solutions was
built on the initial ﬁre development study described in the previous section 6.3. Adjustments
were made to the affected container’s surroundings to represent the geometric proportions of a
13,100 TEU container ship’s cargo hold located amidship. Further, instrumentation was added to
the interior of the container and top of the cargo hold to evaluate temperature increase progress
and smoke dispersion. The following sections were written with the intent of describing the
process and presenting the results of the analysis performed. The FDS-script written to run the
simulation has been made available in appendix A.6.3.
6.4.1
Geometric description
The cargo hold present in ﬁgure 2.4, was utilized in providing the basic geometry of the modeled
cargo hold. Overlay descriptive text, container positions and dimensions was drawn on the ﬁgure
to provide an overview of the case. This can be seen ﬁgure 6.26. Containers left of container
"1,5" were excluded from the simulation, as limited interaction was expected between these and
container "1,2".
==PAGE:122==

cargo container. The affected container has been marked in red.
The overlay diagram served as a plan for implementation of the numbered container into the
simulation. Geometries were implemented with the outside dimensions of typical 40 ft. general
purpose cargo containers. The non-affected containers were left as empty shells consisting of
3 mm steel casing, as they would merely act as obstacles for the smoke. From ﬁgure 6.27 the

7. Conclusions and recommendations
7.1
Objectives, research procedures and achievements
In order to achieve the objectives of this thesis, time was initially spent on putting a perspective
on container ship ﬁres. This section served as the basis for understanding the extensive increase
in container ship sizes and the associated ﬁre related risk. This research was conducted with
main focus on: the evolution of container ships, containerized transport of dangerous goods, ﬁre
detection arrangements, intermodal shipping container ﬁre safety and the ﬁre ﬁghting arrange-
ments aboard the container ships.
1. Describe and understand the causes and development of ﬁres on container ships from a
safety engineering point of view
To describe and understand the causes and development of ﬁres, a ﬁre scenario and hazard
identiﬁcation study was conducted.
Through the course of the ﬁre scenario study, 36 conﬁrmed container ship ﬁres were discovered
by the authors in the period between 1996 - 2017. This was accomplished through extensive
research and qualitative information retrieval from relevant web-based sources combined with
investigations into the European Maritime Safety Agency’s register of maritime casualties.
The aim of the hazard identiﬁcation study was to investigate and classify the substances and/or
articles, which by the authors of this thesis, could be identiﬁed as the main hazards to the
ﬁre safety aboard container ships. This was done through extensive in-depth analysis of the
discovered container ship ﬁre cases and their apparent causes. Hereby the authors were able
to identify containerized shipment of calcium hypochlorite and compressed charcoal briquette
products as being the main hazards to the cargo related ﬁre safety aboard container ships. This
was concluded based on the large fraction of ﬁre incidents, caused by the shipment of these two
types of cargo. Calcium hypochlorite and compressed charcoal briquette products combined
constituted the cause of almost 50% of all the explored ﬁre incidents. It should however be
noted, that the fraction of ﬁres, in which the cause of ﬁre was not possible to uncover by the
authors, represents 19% of the investigated cases. The discovery of these uncovered causes could
therefore further increase the fraction of ﬁres caused by calcium hypochlorite and compressed
==PAGE:128==
128
Chapter 7. Conclusions and recommendations
charcoal briquette products.
Containerized shipment and storage of rechargeable batteries and divinylbenzene was in parallel
concluded as high consequence hazards, due to the severity of the ﬁre incidents and the extensive
damage these cargo ﬁres inﬂicted on both the container ships and their crew. The ﬁndings from
the study therefore lead the authors to conclude, these four cargo types, to constitute the main
hazards to the ﬁre safety aboard container ships. For better understanding the chemical nature of
the identiﬁed hazards and their individual hazardous nature in respect to ﬁre safety, a chemical
study was conducted.
To determine the cause consequence relations associated with the containerized shipment and
storage of the identiﬁed hazards, a cause consequence study was conducted. The study covered
four detailed cause-consequence analyses, where the critical event investigated in the individual
cases was: Decomposition of calcium hypochlorite, thermal runaway in packed charcoal, thermal
runaway in lithium-ion batteries and polymerization of divinylbenzene.
To identify the causes which could provoke the occurrence of these critical events the knowl-
edge attained from the chemical study of the identiﬁed hazards were applied. To conclude
on the ﬁndings, one main parallel could drawn between each of the four identiﬁed hazardous
cargoes, namely their sensitivity to the exposure of heat during containerized storage. When
stored in a general purpose cargo container and exposed to an external heat source each of the
identiﬁed hazardous cargoes are prone to react exothermic through a self escalating runaway
reaction. This reaction has a high probability of causing the cargoes to self-ignite, thereby
causing the initiation of cargo container ﬁres. Therefore an emphasis needs to be put on this
speciﬁc source of danger and how exposing these cargo types to heat, when stored within an
general purpose cargo container, should be avoided. Through the consequence part of the study,
it was possible to explore and identify an event line from the realization of the critical event
to the ﬁnal event for all of the four identiﬁed hazards and how a ﬁre develops through this process.
All the investigated ﬁres, initiated through the storage of the identiﬁed hazards, inﬂicted major
damages and/or destruction of one or more of the stored containers. In some of the cases
extensive damage was also inﬂicted on the container ship. In two of the identiﬁed ﬁre scenarios
human lives were lost. In all scenarios the ship was forced to prematurely terminate its voyage
and require external ﬁre suppression assistance in order to successfully terminate the ﬁre.
In all of the explored ﬁre scenarios the ﬁre detection systems response time were inadequate for
allowing the crew to successfully suppress the cargo container ﬁres. Further, the ﬁre ﬁghting
arrangements themselves were not reliable and not effective in actual suppressing the ﬁres. In
some cases the application of the ﬁreﬁghting arrangements, specially focusing on the container
ships CO2 system and water mist spears, constituted a danger to the safety of the crew and
therefore a risk source.
Based on these ﬁndings it was concluded that the reliability of the current ﬁre ﬁghting ar-
rangements aboard container ships was not sufﬁcient for detecting and suppressing container
ﬁres, when situated to high consequence cargo container ﬁres.
2. Provide a basis for evaluating solutions to the problem of detecting ﬁres
To determine the actual frequency at which cargo ﬁres occur aboard container ships, a fre-
==PAGE:129==
7.2 Limitations of the study
129
quency study was conducted. From the study a best estimate for the overall frequency of
experiencing major ﬁres aboard container ships in the period 2011-2016 was calculated as
6.86·10−4 per ship year, with a 90% chance that the actual frequency was within the interval
4.56·10−4 and 9.94·10−4.
From the attained frequency, it was concluded to proceed with exploring a solution space
of ideas or preliminary concepts which potentially could improve the ﬁre safety aboard container
ships.
To uncover a solution space of ideas which could potentiate the ﬁrst safety barrier in the
protection against ﬁres, namely ﬁre detection, a solution study was conducted. The aim of the
study was to achieve and establish a foundation for creating a number of realistic design concepts,
from which when realized exiting and innovative ﬁre detection products could be developed. All
in the search of improving the overall ﬁre safety aboard container ships.
Through the study the authors were able to achieve a solution space containing seven ideas which
potentially could reduce the response time needed to detect a potential source of ﬁre and thereby
improve the probability of suppressing the ﬁre. By exploring the technologies which created
the basis for the developed solutions, the solution ideas themselves and conducting validation
studies on the explored solutions, two solutions were considered worth further exploring.
[1] The ﬁrst idea was to place temperature sensors in or on all shipping containers.
[2] The second idea was to install a optical smoke detector i all containers.
In the attempt of providing a basis for evaluating the solutions’ actual worth in real-life ﬁre
scenarios, a ﬁre simulation study was performed. The ﬁre simulation study was conducted
through the computational ﬂuid dynamic software Fire Dynamic Simulator. The aim of the study
was to explore how fast a ﬁre could be detected by applying temperature sensors in containers
as opposed to current smoke detection systems in cargo holds. In the simulation, two smoke
detection points were placed in the top part of the cargo hold, in order to represent the actual
placement of a SESD system, which is applied on some container ships for smoke detection.
Further it was explored how placing an optical smoke detector within the container would
improve the response time of detecting smoke and to which degree.
From the study it was discovered that the temperature sensor positioned within the container
was able to detect a temperature increase, approximately 27 seconds after the ignition of the ﬁre.
This constitutes an improvement in detection response time from the SESD system of minimum
100% (Detection interval from SESD detection points: 60 seconds - 74 seconds). It was further
discovered that by placing the optical smoke detector within the container, smoke was detected
within 30 seconds.
7.2
Limitations of the study
An emphasis needs to be put on the time restrictions set for this thesis. The solution space
explored in this paper is only in the very early stages of the exploration process. To be able
to generate actual working design concepts from which real ﬁre detection products could be
generated, a much more thorough investigation would be necessary. This would require extensive
research into the possibilities and capabilities of the solution ideas and technologies, which goes
beyond the scope and objectives of this thesis.
==PAGE:130==
130
Chapter 7. Conclusions and recommendations
From the ﬁre simulation study it was not possible to simulate how a temperature sensor would
monitor the process of a thermal runaway. Attaining this information would be beneﬁcial in the
validation process of the solution, as an early detection in temperature increase could provide the
crew with opportunity of interfering prior self-ignition (depending on the stored cargo)
It was not possible to uncover information on "near-miss" container ﬁres aboard container
ships. By "near-miss" the authors categorizes container ﬁres which development process self-
terminated or were extinguished, prior allowed to emerge into a severe ﬁre incident. Great efforts
was put into attaining this information were various companies was approached combined with
extensive web-based research. Non of the companies were willing to share such information.
By attaining a database on near-misses, it would be possible to explore which barriers actually
worked in detecting and suppressing the cargo container ﬁres.
7.3
Future work
Future work should consider the limitations of this thesis and apply these limitations combined
with the research attained through the study to further investigate and validate the solution space
for improving the ﬁre detection methods aboard container ships. To further improve the safety
barrier of protecting container ships against ﬁres, future work should include exploring a solution
space for improving the suppression arrangements aboard the container ships, based on the
ﬁndings in this thesis.
As mentioned in the introduction the far larger problem is preventing these cargo container
ﬁres. This involves complicated organizational studies and measures, which are beyond the
scope of this thesis and in any case needs to be based on a more thorough understanding of the
ﬁres and ﬁre development.
Future work should therefore focus on both preventing and protecting the container ships
and their crew against these cargo container ﬁres, in the search of improving the overall ﬁre
safety aboard container ships.

A. Appendix
==PAGE:135==
A.1 Minutes from meeting with Øssur Jarleivson Hilduberg, Head of the Board at
DMAIB
135
A.1
Minutes from meeting with Øssur Jarleivson Hilduberg, Head of the Board
at DMAIB
==PAGE:136==
 
2
26. januar 2017 
Sailors firefighting courses vary a bit from country to country. In Denmark (Esbjerg) the course 
participants will typically experience real fire in the training exercises, while this isn’t the case in 
Germany. Here they often use smoke generators instead to simulate the effects of a fire. 
The major problem with CO2 extinguishing systems is that they only remove the oxygen and not the 
heat. Therefore, the fire will often break out again, as soon as oxygen re-enters the cargo space. 
Cargo containers are good at protecting the burning material and allows limited CO2 to reach critical 
areas. Ingen test af CO2 system. Så store problem med anlæggene at folk samles på agterdækket, før 
CO2 anlægget aktiveres.  
Branddetektor levere ikke en løsning på, måske man hellere skulle udvikle en Hvor-er-branden-
henne-melder.  
 
Fires on containerships happen monthly, but limited reporting of these means that there is probably 
a quite great dark figure.  
Examples of dangerous cargo: Lithium batteries, Shisha (water pipe) coal, fireworks 
Goods like these are often required to be transported in reefer containers 
If the crane that transfers cargo from land to ship had IR-cameras mounted for scanning the 
containers to detect containers already on fire, prior to loading them, that might help. Weighing the 
container is mostly done on the lorries that deliver the container to the crane. The actual weight of 
the container isn’t transferred to the ship’s loading computer. The standardisation burden would 
simply be too great. 
The typical attitude towards firefighting. Until now it has been that the crew isn’t doing what they 
should. Model for explaining this: The class societies and SOLAS provides the regulatory framework 
for how much firefighting material and how many fire detection points should be onboard. The 
regulations are however not an expression of best practice.  
DMAIB’s purpose: qualitative data acquisition from crew members, who have experienced fires etc. 
It is not to do statistics. Do to the low frequency of big fires, there isn’t enough data. DMAIB 
generally work on cases that can be categorised as high impact, low probability. 
Fire detectors in the engine room are also used for detecting leaking high-pressure fuel lines, as they 
can detect particles as well.  
The ordinary model for determining what fire suppression equipment to have onboard is based on a 
megawatt model (how much water this would require is then calculated). Foam is effective for pool 
fires, i.e. burning liquids.     
Risk managers at the ship owner’s offices have been caught off guard, and are unsure how to tackle 
this development. 
Stopping undeclared dangerous goods coming aboard the ship seems virtually impossible. 
Tracking of containers (prior to being loaded) is virtually impossible.  
 
One container cycle: Maersk doesn’t know where the container came from, BlueWater 
shipping know a bit more about its origins, while the brokers responsible for booking the 
container knows a bit more again.  
==PAGE:137==
 
3
26. januar 2017 
 
IBM had a cooperation with Maersk to improve the trackability of the individual containers 
via a tracking chip, it didn’t succeed. 
In relation to the new water mist spears: 
- 
You should always be careful with the discrepancy between what a person wants to use a 
piece of equipment for and how it should be used. Considering an angle grinder; it doesn’t 
necessarily make clear sense how it should be used.  
Water expands 1700 times when it is turned into steam. What does this do to a cargo container and 
the people surrounding it, when the water mist spear is introduced?  
Considering containers stacked high, even with the new rules on water pressure the beam still bends 
over a distance. 
Temperature detection would be a substantial tool. Now, we detect via smoke and not temperature. 
Unfortunately, IR-cameras can’t handle the treatment they are receiving. Also, they have a tough 
time distinguishing between an actual fire and the heat coming for the HFO-tanks at the bottom of 
the cargo space.
